Refutation of God's Speech as Created in Others
The author refutes the view that God's speech is created in another and quotes Ibn ʿArabī.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the view that God's speech is created in something other than God leads to absurd consequences and is therefore invalid.

Core Idea
God's speech cannot be created in another without attributing falsehood to Him.

The author presents a strong rational argument against those who claim that God speaks by creating speech in other beings. Using the Quranic verse where skins say 'Allah caused us to speak' (not 'Allah spoke'), he shows the clear distinction. Then he warns that such a view would lead to attributing all kinds of corrupt speech to God, which is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the logical flow from question to conclusion.
2
Understand the key distinction
Identify the difference between 'nṭq' (speaking) and 'anṭaqa' (causing to speak) as used in the Quran.
The verse shows that the skins said 'Allah caused us to speak', not 'Allah spoke'.
3
Analyze the reductio ad absurdum
The author shows that if all created speech were God's speech, then falsehood, lies, and blasphemy would be attributed to God.
This is an impossible consequence.
4
Reflect on the quote from Ibn 'Arabi
The author quotes Ibn 'Arabi's extreme statement that all speech is God's speech, and then dismisses it.
The author concludes with 'Exalted is Allah above that'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكيف يصح أن يكون متكلماً بكلام يقوم بغيره، ولو صح ذلك للزم أن يكون ما أحدثه من الكلام في الجمادات والحيوانات كلامه ولا يفرق بين نطق وأنطق الله، وإنما قالت الجلود: انْطَقَنَا اللهُ ﴾ [فصلت: ۲۱] ، ولم تقل نطق الله ، بل يلزم أن يكون متكلماً بكل كلام خلقه في غيره زوراً كان أو كذباً أو كفراً أو هذياناً، تعالى الله عن ذلك. قال القونوي وقد طرد ذلك الإتحادية، فقال ابن عربي: وكل كلام في الوجود كلامه سواء علينا نثره ونظامه
And how is it valid that He is a speaker by speech that subsists in other than Him? If that were correct, it would necessitate that whatever speech He brings about in inanimate objects and animals would be His speech, and no distinction would be made between 'speaking' (nṭq) and 'causing to speak' (anṭaqa). Indeed, the skins said: 'Allah caused us to speak' (Faṣṣilat 41:21), and they did not say 'Allah spoke'. Rather, it would necessitate that He is a speaker by every speech He creates in other than Him, whether falsehood, lie, disbelief, or nonsense. Exalted is Allah above that. Al-Qūnavī said: 'The Ittiḥādiyya (monists) followed this [logic], and Ibn ʿArabī said: "And every speech in existence is His speech, equally to us its prose and its poetry."'
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage refutes the notion that God's speech is a created entity that subsists in something other than God. The author argues that if that were the case, then any speech God creates in an inanimate object or animal would be attributable to God as His speech, blurring the distinction between God speaking directly and causing others to speak. He cites the Quranic verse where the skins of the damned say 'Allah caused us to speak' (anṭaqanā Allāh), not 'Allah spoke' (naṭaqa Allāh). This shows that there is a difference. Moreover, if all created speech were God's speech, then falsehood, blasphemy, and nonsense would be attributed to God. The author then reports that al-Qūnavī mentioned that the Ittiḥādiyya (monistic thinkers) took this view to its extreme, and Ibn ʿArabī stated that every speech in existence is God's speech. The author clearly rejects this, concluding 'Exalted is Allah above that.'

Key Terms
نطق
أنطق
كلام
إتحادية
ابن عربي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's speech is created in other beings and can be rightfully called His speech.
Correction: If that were the case, then all created speech, including falsehood and blasphemy, would be God's speech, and the distinction between 'speaking' and 'causing to speak' would be lost. The Quran maintains the distinction.

Checkpoint
What did the skins say according to the Quran?
A) Allah spoke
B) Allah caused us to speak
C) We speak
D) We are silent

Key Takeaway
God's speech is not created in others; attributing created speech to God leads to absurdities and is rejected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believe that every speech in existence — including lies, blasphemy, and slander — is God's speech, how would that affect your relationship with Him? Would you still be able to call upon Him as the All-Truthful? What does this lesson's refutation say about the seriousness of attributing any form of imperfection to God?
?
Read the Quranic verse where the skins say 'Allah caused us to speak' (41:21). Have you ever thought about the difference between God speaking directly and God causing something to speak? How does this distinction protect your reverence for the Qur'an as His uncreated words? Pause and ask yourself: do I treat the Qur'an as if it were merely a created text like any other, or as the eternal speech of my Lord?
?
The author argues that if created speech were God's speech, then nonsense and falsehood would be attributed to Him. Is there any area in your life where you lazily attribute something to God without proper evidence — like blaming Him for your own mistakes? Write down one such area and commit to correcting it today.

intermediate
6 min
The Three-Fold Disjunction: Debate with Bishr al-Marisi
Relates debate where Abdul Aziz compels Bishr with three impossible options for created speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument used to prove that the Quran is an uncreated attribute of Allah, by eliminating three possible ways it could be created.

Core Idea
Createdness of the Quran is impossible because a locus for accidents cannot be Allah.

In this passage, we witness a powerful theological debate between Imam Abdul Aziz al-Makki and the Mu'tazilite Bishr al-Marisi before Caliph al-Ma'mun. The Imam presents a three-fold disjunction, forcing Bishr to accept that the Quran must be an eternal attribute, not a created thing. This lesson unpacks the logic and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the story and understand the stakes
The debate takes place before the caliph. Bishr threatens the Imam, but the Imam remains calm and presents a logical dilemma.
2
Identify the three options presented
Notice the three possibilities: creation in Himself, creation in another, or creation as a self-subsisting entity. Each is examined and shown impossible.
3
Understand why each option is impossible
Follow the reasoning: Allah cannot be a locus of accidents, speech created in another would be His speech, and speech needs a speaker.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since all three creation scenarios are impossible, the Quran must be an eternal attribute of Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال القونوي: وقد طرد ذلك الإتحادية، فقال ابن عربي:
وكل كلام في الوجود كلامه سواء علينا نثره ونظامه
وبمثل ذلك ألزم الإمام عبد العزيز المكي بشر المريسي بين يدي المأمون بعد أن تكلم ملتزماً أن لا يخرج عن نص التنزيل وألزمه الحجة فقال بشر: يا أمير المؤمنين! ليدع مطالبتي بنص التنزيل ويناظرني بغيره فإن لم يدع قوله ويرجع عنه ويقر بخلق القرآن الساعة وإلا فدمي حلال. قال عبد العزيز: تسألني أو أسألك؟ فقال بشر: أنت، وطمع في، قال: فقلت له: يلزمك واحدة من ثلاث لابد منها: إما أن تقول إن الله خلق القرآن في نفسه، أو خلقه قائماً بذاته ونفسه أو خلقه في غيره قال: أقول خلقه كما خلق الأشياء كلها وحاد عن الجواب، فقال المأمون: اشرح أنت هذه المسألة ودع بشراً، فقد انقطع. فقال عبد العزيز: إن قال خلق كلامه في نفسه فهذا محال؛ لأن الله لا يكون محلاً للحوادث ولا يكون منه شيء مخلوقاً وإن قال: خلقه في غيره فيلزمه في النظر والقياس أن كل كلام خلقه الله في غيره فهو كلامه، وإن قال: خلقه قائماً بنفسه وذاته فهذا محال؛ لأن الكلام لا يكون إلا من متكلم كما لا تكون الإرادة إلا من مريد، ولا العلم إلا من عالم، ولا يعقل كلام قائم بنفسه يتكلم بذاته، فلما استحال من هذه الجهات أن يكون مخلوقاً علم أنه صفة الله. هذا مختصر من كلام الإمام عبد العزيز في "الجيدة".
Al-Qunawi said: The Ittihadiyya (pantheists) carried that to its extreme, and Ibn Arabi said: 'And every speech in existence is His speech, whether its prose or its system, to us is the same.' And by something similar, Imam Abdul Aziz al-Makki compelled Bishr al-Marisi in the presence of al-Ma'mun, after he (Bishr) spoke insisting that he would not depart from the text of revelation and the proof compelled him. Bishr said: 'O Commander of the Faithful! Let him cease demanding from me the text of revelation and debate me with something else. If he does not stop his statement and retract and acknowledge the createdness of the Qur'an this instant, then my blood is lawful.' Abdul Aziz said: 'Do you ask me or do I ask you?' Bishr said: 'You (ask).' And he was hopeful. He (Abdul Aziz) said: So I said to him: One of three options necessarily binds you, no alternative: either you say that Allah created the Qur'an in Himself, or He created it subsisting by His essence and Himself, or He created it in another. He said: 'I say He created it as He created all things' and deviated from the answer. Al-Ma'mun said: 'You explain this matter and leave Bishr, for he has been refuted.' So Abdul Aziz said: If he says He created His speech in Himself, that is impossible because Allah is not a locus for accidents (mahall li l-hawadith) and nothing created comes from Him. And if he says He created it in another, it would necessarily follow in theory and analogy that every speech Allah creates in another is His speech. And if he says He created it subsisting by itself and its essence, that is impossible because speech only exists from a speaker, just as will only exists from a willer, and knowledge only from a knower, and a speech subsisting by itself speaking by its essence is inconceivable. So since it is impossible from these aspects that it be created, it is known that it is an attribute of Allah. This is a summary of the words of Imam Abdul Aziz in 'al-Jayyidah'.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage first quotes al-Qunawi, who notes that the Ittihadiyya (pantheistic monists) carried the Mu'tazilite position to its logical extreme, as expressed in Ibn Arabi's line equating all speech with Allah's speech. Then it relates a famous debate: Imam Abdul Aziz al-Makki confronted Bishr al-Marisi, a leading Mu'tazilite, before Caliph al-Ma'mun. Bishr initially insisted on sticking to revelation but then threatened that if Abdul Aziz did not recant, he would demand his blood. Abdul Aziz offered a three-fold disjunction: either Allah created the Quran in Himself (impossible because Allah cannot be a locus of temporal events), or in another (then all speech created in another would be Allah's, leading to absurdities like falsehood being Allah's speech), or as a self-subsistent entity (impossible because speech requires a speaker). Bishr evaded by saying 'as He created all things,' but the caliph declared him refuted. Abdul Aziz then explained the three impossibilities, concluding that since creation is impossible from all angles, speech must be an eternal attribute of Allah.

Key Terms
اتحادية
ابن عربي
بشر المريسي
عبد العزيز المكي
ثلاث جهات
خلق القرآن
محل للحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran is created because all things are created.
Correction: The Quran cannot be created because it is an attribute of Allah, and attributes are not created. The three-fold disjunction shows that any attempt to place creation leads to absurdities.

Checkpoint
Why did Caliph al-Ma'mun declare that Bishr al-Marisi had been refuted?
A) Bishr gave an evasive answer instead of choosing one of the three options
B) Bishr admitted that the Quran is created
C) Bishr remained silent
D) Bishr agreed with Abdul Aziz

Key Takeaway
The three-fold disjunction demonstrates that the Quran cannot be created, establishing it as an eternal attribute of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before reading this passage, had you ever truly pondered the logical implications of believing the Quran is created? In what ways might you have unconsciously accepted a flawed premise about Allah's speech? Today, commit to examining your own assumptions about the attributes of Allah with the same rigor as Abdul Aziz.
?
This passage shows Bishr evading the truth even in the presence of the caliph. Are there areas in your life where you avoid a clear truth because it demands a change in your belief or behavior? What truth about Allah's attributes are you reluctant to fully embrace, and what step will you take today to submit to it?
?
The argument refutes not only the createdness of the Quran but also the pantheistic extreme. Have you ever diluted your reverence for the Quran by treating it as a mere temporal book rather than the eternal speech of Allah? How will you change your daily interaction with the Quran to reflect this reality?

Refuting Those Who Deny Reality
Refutes sophists and their followers by appealing to observable creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the observable world refutes the claims of skeptics who deny objective reality.

Core Idea
Observable creation directly refutes all denial of reality.

The author now moves from positive affirmations to refuting those who deny the reality of the created world. He points to the evident existence of the heavens and earth as a direct rebuttal to the Sophists and their followers, who claim all is illusion. This passage grounds Islamic certainty in the undeniable observable order.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the key groups mentioned: the Sophists, existentialists, and incarnationists.
The passage is short but dense with references.
2
Identify the argument
The argument is simple: the observable reality of the earth and sky refutes those who deny the reality of things. Understand why this is a powerful refutation.
The author uses 'as observed' (كما هو مشاهد) as the proof.
3
Consider the groups refuted
List the groups: Sophists (skeptics who deny external reality), atheistic existentialists (deny a Creator), incarnationists (deify creation or hold indwelling beliefs), and ignorant Sufis who follow similar ideas.
These are heresies that Islamic theology must address.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal importance
Why does it matter that things have real, objective existence? It affirms the Creator's power and the trustworthiness of our senses, which are needed for theological knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم في تحقق الأشياء كما هو مشاهد في الأرض والسماء ردّ على السوفسطائية ومن تبعهم من أهل الأهواء حيث ينكرون حقائق الأشياء ويزعمون أنها أوهام وخيالات كالأحلام، ويقرب منه الوجودية الإلحادية والحلولية وأمثالهم من جهلة الصوفية.
Then regarding the reality of things, as observed in the earth and heavens, it is a refutation of the Sophists (as-Sufisṭā'iyya) and those who follow them from the people of desires, since they deny the realities of things and claim that they are illusions and fantasies like dreams, and close to them are the atheistic existentialists (al-wujūdiyya al-ilḥādiyya) and the incarnationists (al-ḥulūliyya) and their likes from the ignorant Sufis.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal refutation. The author points to the observable reality of things in the earth and heavens as evidence against skeptics (the Sophists) who deny the reality of external objects, claiming they are mere illusions. The author also includes modern-like heretical groups: the 'existentialists' who deny a creator and the 'incarnationists' who believe in divine indwelling, comparing them to ignorant Sufis. The argument is that the clear, observable existence of the created world refutes all such denials of reality.

Key Terms
السوفسطائية
حقائق الأشياء
الوجودية الإلحادية
الحلولية
أهل الأهواء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this refutation is against all forms of philosophical skepticism or only against ancient Greeks.
Correction: The passage specifically refutes those who deny the objective reality of creation in order to deny the Creator or to promote pantheistic ideas, such as the Sophists, atheistic existentialists, and incarnationists.

Checkpoint
Which groups are refuted in this passage?
A) The Mu'tazila and Qadariyya.
B) The Sophists, existentialists, and incarnationists.
C) The Maturidis and Ash'aris.
D) The Zoroastrians and dualists.

Key Takeaway
The evident existence of the heavens and earth is a direct refutation of all who deny the objective reality of creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you used the excuse that 'nothing is real' to avoid facing a difficult truth about yourself? What would it take for you to stand firm on the reality of what Allah has created, even when it is uncomfortable?
?
Which of your own desires (ahwā') might be subtly leading you to deny a truth that is as clear as the earth under your feet? What concrete step will you take today to align your perception with reality instead of desire?
?
The author warns that even some who claim spirituality ( ignorant Sufis) can fall into denial. What safeguards have you built into your own spiritual practice to ensure it is grounded in truth and not mere imagination? If you have none, what will you set in place this week?

Refutation of the Mu'tazila: Distinguishing Decree from Decreed
Refutes the Mu'tazila objection with a distinction between decree and decreed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni refutation of the Mu'tazila argument that if kufr is by God's decree, then one must be pleased with it, and how the distinction between decree (qada) and decreed (maqdi) resolves the objection.

Core Idea
Unbelief is God's decree, but we disapprove of the decreed, not the decree.

The Mu'tazila raised a powerful objection against the Sunni position that all actions, including unbelief, are by God's decree. They argued that if unbelief is decreed, then being pleased with it becomes obligatory—but that is itself unbelief. In this passage, the author refutes the objection with a crucial distinction between the decree itself and the thing decreed, showing that our obligation is to be pleased with God's judgment, not with the sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila objection
Read the first part of the passage carefully, which presents the Mu'tazila argument in their own words. Notice the logical chain: if kufr is by decree, then pleasure is obligatory; but pleasure in kufr is kufr; therefore kufr is not by decree.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the refutation and the key distinction
The author refutes by stating 'unbelief is decreed, not the decree' (الكفر مقضي لا قضاء). Grasp this distinction: the decree (qada) is God's act of judging and willing; the decreed (maqdi) is the content. Our duty is to be pleased with the former, not necessarily the latter.
This distinction is central to Sunni theology on divine decree and human responsibility.
3
Reflect on the clarification
Read the clarification explaining that unbelief has a relation to God as His creation according to wisdom, and He is the absolute owner who is not harmed. Consider why this defense matters for affirming both God's sovereignty and human accountability.
The passage cuts off, but the main argument is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قول المعتزلة: «لو كان الكفر بقضاء الله تعالى لوجب الرضا به لأن الرضا بالقضاء واجب، واللازم باطل؛ لأن الرضا بالكفر كفر، فثبت أن الكفر ليس بقضاء الله، فلم تكن جميع أفعال العباد بقضاء الله تعالى على ما ذهب إليه أهل السنة والجماعة»، فمدفوع بأن الكفر مقضي لا قضاء، والرضى إنما يجب بالقضاء دون المقضى. وتوضيحه : أن الكفر له نسبة إليه سبحانه وهي كونه خلقه على مقتضى حكمته ولا اعتراض عليه فى مشيئته، فإنه مالك الملك يتصرف فيه كيف يشاء، لا يتضرر بشيء كما لا
As for the saying of the Mu'tazila: 'If unbelief (kufr) were by the decree (qaḍā') of Allah Almighty, then being pleased with it would be obligatory, because being pleased with the decree is obligatory, but the necessary consequence is false, because being pleased with unbelief is unbelief; so it is established that unbelief is not by Allah's decree, and thus not all actions of servants are by Allah's decree as the Ahl al-Sunnah wal-Jama'ah hold.' So it is refuted by saying that unbelief is decreed (maqḍī), not the decree (qaḍā'), and pleasure is only obligatory with the decree, not with the decreed. And its clarification: that unbelief has a relation to Him, glorified, which is that He created it according to His wisdom, and there is no objection to His will, for He is the Owner of the Kingdom, disposing of it as He wills; He is not harmed by anything, just as...
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu'tazila objection. The Mu'tazila argue: If unbelief (kufr) were by God's decree, then one must be pleased with it, because being pleased with God's decree is obligatory. But being pleased with kufr is itself kufr, so the premise is false, meaning kufr is not by God's decree, hence not all actions are decreed. The Sunni refutation distinguishes between the decree (qada) and the decreed (maqdi). What is obligatory is to be pleased with the decree itself—that is, God's judgment and wisdom—not with the decreed content (kufr). Thus, one is pleased that God decrees as He wills, while disapproving of the evil of kufr. The author adds a clarification: Kufr is related to God as His creation according to His wisdom, and no one can object to His will, as He is the absolute Owner. The text is cut off, but the argument is clear.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الكفر
القضاء
المقضي
الرضا بالقضاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That being pleased with God's decree means approving of everything decreed, including sin.
Correction: The author shows the distinction between decree (qada) and decreed (maqdi). Our duty is to be pleased with the decree itself (God's act of judging and willing) but not necessarily with the decreed content (which may be evil). Thus, we can accept God's decree while disapproving of unbelief.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the Mu'tazila argument that if kufr is by God's decree, one must be pleased with it?
A) By denying that kufr is from God.
B) By distinguishing between the decree (qada) and the decreed (maqdi).
C) By arguing that pleasure with kufr is not kufr.
D) By claiming that God's decree is not obligatory.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni response to the Mu'tazila objection hinges on the distinction between God's decree (which we must accept) and the decreed content (which we may disapprove), preserving both divine sovereignty and moral responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a specific event in your life that you struggled to accept as part of God's decree—an illness, loss, or failure. Can you now see how you can be pleased with God's decree (qaḍā') while still hating the event itself (maqḍī)? What concrete step will you take tonight to practice this distinction in your heart?
?
When you hear about a tragedy in the world, do you find yourself questioning God's justice? How does this lesson's distinction help you reconcile God's wisdom with the presence of evil? Write down one honest doubt you have and, without avoiding it, re-read the source's point that 'He is the Owner of the Kingdom, disposing of it as He wills.' What does that demand of you?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that if unbelief is decreed, then being pleased with it is obligatory—and they concluded that unbelief cannot be decreed. Have you ever fallen into a similar logical trap, thinking that because you disapprove of something, it cannot come from God? How will you guard your tongue and heart the next time you are tempted to deny divine decree over an evil you hate?

God's Sovereignty and Refutation of Dualism
Conclusion that God is not questioned and refutation of dualist sects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author equates the Qadariyya and Mu'tazila to polytheists, and how they are compared to the Magians.

Core Idea
Denying God's exclusive creation of actions leads to shirk.

This passage concludes the discussion on God's sole creative power. It draws a stark implication: anyone who claims the servant creates his own actions is a polytheist, not a monotheist. The author supports this with a hadith comparing the Qadariyya to the Magians, and goes further to condemn the Mu'tazila as worse than the Magians.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the Quranic foundation
Read the verse from Al-Anbiya:23 which states that God is not questioned about His actions. Understand that this establishes God's absolute sovereignty and that nothing is obligatory upon Him from His creatures.
The verse is a proof that God's actions are not subject to human evaluation.
2
Analyze the hadith and its implication
The hadith 'The Qadariyya are the Magians of this nation' likens those who affirm human creation of actions to the dualists. Understand why: the Magians believed in two creators (God and Satan), while the Qadariyya effectively make every human a partner in creation.
The comparison is severe because it classifies Qadariyya as polytheists.
3
Examine the Transoxiana scholars' condemnation
The passage notes that scholars of Transoxiana declared the Mu'tazila worse than the Magians. Consider why: Magians only affirmed one partner (Satan), while Mu'tazila, by denying predestination, affirm countless partners (every human).
This shows the extreme gravity of the issue in classical Sunni thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا تحقق أن الله خالق الخلق علم أنه لا يجب لهم شيء على الحق فإنه سبحانه: ﴿ لا يُسْأَلُ عَمَّا يَفْعَلُ وَهُمْ يُسْأَلُونَ ﴾ [الأنبياء: ٢٣]، وكان القياس أن يقال: القائل بكون العبد خالقا لأفعاله يكون من المشركين دون الموحدين، كما يشير إليه حديث: ((القدرية مجوس هذه الأمة)). حيث ذهبوا إلى أن للعالم فاعلين: أحدهما الله سبحانه وتعالى وهو فاعل الخير، والثاني الشيطان وهو فاعل الشر. قال: ولذا بالغ مشايخ ما وراء النهر مبالغة في تضليل المعتزلة حتى قالوا: «إنهم أقبح من المجوس حيث لم يثبتوا إلا شريكا واحدا، والمعتزلة أثبتوا شركاء لا تحصى». ولكن المحققين...
And when it is established that Allah is the Creator of creation, it is known that nothing is obligatory upon Him for them, for He, Exalted is He: { He is not questioned about what He does, but they will be questioned } [Al-Anbiya: 23]. And the logical conclusion is to say: The one who says that the servant is the creator of his actions is among the polytheists, not among the monotheists, as indicated by the hadith: 'The Qadariyya are the Magians of this nation.' Whereby they held that the world has two agents: one is Allah, Exalted is He, the agent of good, and the second is Satan, the agent of evil. He said: Therefore the scholars of Transoxiana went to great lengths in declaring the Mu'tazila to be astray, until they said: 'They are worse than the Magians because the Magians only affirmed one partner, while the Mu'tazila affirmed countless partners.' But the researchers...
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage draws a theological conclusion. Since Allah is the sole Creator, nothing is due to Him from His creatures—He is not questioned about His actions (Al-Anbiya: 23). The author argues that anyone who claims the servant creates his own actions is a polytheist (mushrik), not a monotheist (muwahhid). This is supported by the hadith: 'The Qadariyya are the Magians of this nation.' The Qadariyya believed in two agents: God for good and Satan for evil. The scholars of Transoxiana (Central Asia) went even further, declaring the Mu'tazila worse than the Magians because the Magians only affirmed one partner (Satan), whereas the Mu'tazila, by denying that God creates all human actions, effectively make every human a partner in creation. The passage ends abruptly with 'But the researchers...'.

Key Terms
قدرية (qadariyya)
مجوس (majus / Magians)
مشرك (mushrik / polytheist)
موحد (muwahhid / monotheist)
معتزلة (mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming that humans have free will and create their own actions does not constitute shirk (polytheism).
Correction: The author argues that it does, because it assigns a share in creation to creatures, thus violating God's exclusive sovereignty as Creator.

Checkpoint
To whom are the Qadariyya compared in the hadith cited?
A) Christians
B) Jews
C) Magians
D) Atheists

Key Takeaway
The belief that humans create their own actions is equated with polytheism and is comparable to the dualism of the Magians, with the Mu'tazila considered even worse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed in a task or avoid a sin, do you ever feel a sense of pride as if you alone accomplished it, forgetting that Allah is the sole Creator of your actions and even your ability to act? How can you train your heart to attribute every moment of obedience — and every slip — back to His will and power, so that you never fall into the subtle shirk of self-creation?
?
The hadith warns that those who deny Allah's exclusive creation of actions are like the Magians. In your daily life, do you sometimes mentally 'split' the world into what you think you control and what Allah controls, as if there are two independent realms? What specific thoughts or habits can you change today to confess that Allah is the only agent, even in your own choices?
?
The Mu'tazila are considered worse because they multiply partners. Think of the people, talents, or circumstances you tend to credit for your achievements — have you made them 'partners' with Allah in your mind? If so, how can you repent from this hidden shirk and sincerely proclaim that nothing happens except by His will and creation?

The Qadariyya as the Magians of This Ummah
Commentator equates those who claim the slave creates his own actions with polytheists using the hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why claiming humans create their own actions is equated with shirk and the Magian belief in two creators.

Core Idea
Attributing independent creation to humans mirrors Magian dualism and constitutes shirk.

This passage from the commentary on Fiqh al-Akbar presents a striking analogy: those who attribute independent creative power to human beings are likened to polytheists. The author supports this with a Prophetic hadith that the Qadariyya are the Magians of this ummah. We will unpack the reasoning behind this comparison.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note the logical progression from claim to hadith to explanation.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage begins with an analogy: the one who says the slave creates his own actions is among the polytheists, not the monotheists. Understand why this is a serious theological charge.
The claim is not just an opinion but a matter of tawhid.
3
Understand the hadith reference
The author cites the hadith 'al-Qadariyya majus hadhihi al-ummah.' Research who the Magians were—they believed in two ultimate principles: a good god (light) and an evil god (darkness).
The Qadariyya are not literally Magians but resemble them in their dualistic view of creation.
4
Reflect on the theological error
The passage explains that the Qadariyya held that the universe has two agents: Allah (doer of good) and Satan (doer of evil). Consider why this implicitly denies Allah's sole lordship and sovereignty.
Even though they affirm Allah as creator of good, attributing independent creation of evil to Satan or humans is a form of shirk.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان القياس أن يقال: القائل بكون العبد خالقا لأفعاله يكون من المشركين دون الموحدين، كما يشير إليه حديث: ((القدرية مجوس هذه الأمة)). (٢) حيث ذهبوا إلى أن للعالم فاعلين: أحدهما الله سبحانه وتعالى وهو فاعل الخير، والثاني الشيطان وهو فاعل الشر.
And the analogy would be to say: The one who says that the slave is the creator of his actions is among the polytheists, not the monotheists, as indicated by the hadith: 'The Qadariyya are the Magians of this ummah.' (2) Because they held that the universe has two agents: one is Allah the Exalted and He is the doer of good, and the second is Satan who is the doer of evil.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents an argument by analogy (qiyas). If one claims that the slave independently creates his own actions, that is like shirk because it posits a second creator alongside God. The commentator cites a hadith that the Qadariyya (those who deny divine decree regarding actions) are like the Magians (Zoroastrians) who believed in two ultimate principles: a good god and an evil god. The Qadariyya effectively assign the creation of evil actions to the slave, making a dualistic system, thus they are considered polytheists in this sense.

Key Terms
قدرية
مجوس
مشركون
موحدون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith means that Qadariyya are completely outside Islam and are like Zoroastrians in religion.
Correction: The hadith does not remove them from Islam; it compares them to Magians in their dualistic tendency, not in actual faith. They are Muslims who hold a grave theological error.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited, the Qadariyya are compared to which group?
A) Christians
B) Jews
C) Magians
D) Atheists

Key Takeaway
The Qadariyya are likened to Magians because both posit two independent creators, thus falling into shirk.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself acting as if you have ultimate control over your successes and failures, forgetting that every action and its outcome depends solely on Allah's will? How does this mindset resemble the Qadariyya's error?
?
If you truly believe that Allah is the sole creator of your actions, then why do you sometimes rely on your own strength rather than begging Him for help? In what specific situation today will you consciously affirm His exclusive control?
?
When you sin, do you blame yourself in a way that denies Allah's sovereignty over that act? Or do you accept full responsibility while still recognizing that the act occurred only by His permission? Which stance affirms tawheed, and which leads to subtle dualism?

The Mu'tazila: Between Extreme Condemnation and Scholarly Vindication
Two scholarly views on Mu'tazila's Islamic status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two contrasting scholarly positions on the Mu'tazila regarding their status within Islam and the reasoning behind the muhaqqiqun's vindication.

Core Idea
Not all scholars declared the Mu'tazila disbelievers; the muhaqqiqun considered them Muslims.

This passage presents a critical debate in Islamic theology: the status of the Mu'tazila. Some scholars from Transoxiana went so far as to say the Mu'tazila are worse than the Magians, but the verifying scholars (muhaqqiqun) disagreed, affirming that the Mu'tazila are still part of the Islamic community. Let's examine the arguments behind both views.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the two distinct positions mentioned.
The passage contains a strong claim from Transoxiana scholars and a counter from the muhaqqiqun.
2
Identify the two views
The first view is that Mu'tazila are worse than Magians. The second is that they are among the sects of Islam. Underline the reasons given for each.
The muhaqqiqun interpret the harsh statement as a deterrent.
3
Analyze the reasoning of the muhaqqiqun
Understand why the muhaqqiqun do not consider Mu'tazila guilty of shirk. They argue Mu'tazila do not make the slave an independent creator, but attribute creation to God in essence and to the slave through means.
This distinction avoids both shirk in divinity and worship of idols.
4
Reflect on the lesson's core idea
Consider how this passage shows that even within Sunni scholarship, there is disagreement on takfir, and that the muhaqqiqun take a more inclusive stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ولذا بالغ مشايخ ما وراء النهر مبالغة في تضليل المعتزلة حتى قالوا: «إنهم أقبح من المجوس حيث لم يثبتوا إلا شريكا واحدا، والمعتزلة أثبتوا شركاء لا تحصى». ولكن المحققين على أن المعتزلة من طوائف الإسلام، وحملوا ما ذكر على الزجر للأنام، لأنهم لم يجعلوا العبد خالقاً بالاستقلال بل يقولون : إنه سبحانه خالق بالذات والعبد خالق بواسطة الأسباب والآلات التي خلقها الله تعالى في العبد، ولم يثبتوا الإشراك بالحقيقة وهو إثبات الشريك في الألوهية كالمجوس، ولا بمعنى استحقاق العبادة كعبدة الأصنام.
He said: Therefore the shaykhs of Transoxiana went to extremes in declaring the Mu'tazila to be misguided until they said: 'They are worse than the Magians, for the Magians only affirmed one partner (two gods), while the Mu'tazila affirmed countless partners.' But the verifying scholars (muhaqqiqun) hold that the Mu'tazila are among the sects of Islam, and they interpret what was mentioned as a deterrent for the people, because they (the Mu'tazila) did not make the slave an independent creator, but rather they say: He (God) is the Creator in essence, and the slave is a creator through the means and instruments that God has created in him, and they did not establish polytheism in reality, which is affirming a partner in divinity like the Magians, nor in the sense of deserving worship like the idol worshippers.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents two views on the Mu'tazila. First, some scholars from beyond the river (Transoxiana) went to extremes in declaring Mu'tazila to be misguided, even saying they are worse than Magians because Magians only had two gods whereas Mu'tazila posit many creators (every human creates his own actions). However, the verifying scholars (muhaqqiqun) hold that Mu'tazila are still part of the Islamic community. They argue that Mu'tazila did not attribute independent creation to the slave; rather, they say God creates the essence and the slave creates through the means and instruments that God has placed in him. Therefore, Mu'tazila are not guilty of actual shirk, whether the shirk of associating a partner in divinity (like Magians) or the shirk of worshipping other than God (like idol worshippers). Thus the severe statements were meant as a deterrent, not as a factual declaration of excommunication.

Key Terms
معتزلة
محققون
إشراك
مجوس
عبدة الأصنام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All scholars agreed on takfir (declaring disbelief) of the Mu'tazila.
Correction: The muhaqqiqun (verifying scholars) considered the Mu'tazila to be among the sects of Islam and did not declare them disbelievers.

Checkpoint

A) They believe God is the sole creator of all things
B) They do not make the slave an independent creator
C) They deny the existence of Satan
D) They accept all hadith

Key Takeaway
The muhaqqiqun affirmed that the Mu'tazila remain within Islam because they do not attribute independent creative power to humans, thus avoiding real shirk.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a fellow Muslim from the fold of Islam because of a theological disagreement? How does the muhaqqiqun's approach of treating the Mu'tazila as Muslims challenge you to reconsider your own judgments? Will you examine your heart and repent from any unjust takfir?
?
The muhaqqiqun interpreted the harsh statement of the Transoxiana scholars as a deterrent for the people. When you see a fellow Muslim falling into error, do your first thoughts lean towards condemnation or towards giving them the benefit of the doubt? What steps can you take today to adopt the more merciful approach of the verifying scholars?
?
This passage shows that even those who attribute creation to humans (in a qualified sense) are not necessarily polytheists. Are you quick to label others as committing shirk without understanding their full position? How can you cultivate humility and avoid hasty judgments in matters of creed?

Mu'tazila: Islamic Sect and Their View on Human Action
Clarifies Mu'tazila's sectarian status and their qualified view of human creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila are considered within Islam and their nuanced position on the creation of human actions.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila affirm God as sole creator, but assign human a derived creative role via God-given means.

This passage addresses a critical theological question: Are the Mu'tazila still part of Islam despite their view that humans create their own actions? The author clarifies that their position does not amount to shirk, because they see human creation as dependent on God. Let's read the text carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words come through.
The passage is short, so take your time with each phrase.
2
Identify the key claim about sectarian status
Notice that the passage begins by affirming that Mu'tazila are among the sects of Islam. This is a significant statement given their controversial views.
3
Understand their view on human creation
The passage states that Mu'tazila do not say the servant is an independent creator. Instead, they say the servant creates through the means and instruments God created in him.
This is a qualified creation – not independent.
4
Note how they avoid shirk
The author explains that Mu'tazila do not establish shirk in reality – not like the Magians who believe in two creators, nor like idol worshippers.
Thus their view is within the bounds of Islamic monotheism.
5
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why this distinction matters: It shows that not every claim of human action creation amounts to associating partners with God.
The key is the dependence on God's provision of means.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن المعتزلة من طوائف الإسلام، وحملوا ما ذكر على الزجر للأنام، لأنهم لم يجعلوا العبد خالقاً بالاستقلال بل يقولون : إنه سبحانه خالق بالذات والعبد خالق بواسطة الأسباب والآلات التي خلقها الله تعالى في العبد، ولم يثبتوا الإشراك بالحقيقة وهو إثبات الشريك في الألوهية كالمجوس، ولا بمعنى استحقاق العبادة كعبدة الأصنام.
That the Mu'tazila are among the sects of Islam, and they interpreted what was mentioned as a deterrent for the people, because they did not make the servant a creator independently, but rather they say: He (God) is Creator by essence, and the servant is a creator through the means and instruments that God created in the servant, and they did not establish association (shirk) in reality, which is establishing a partner in divinity like the Magians, nor in the sense of deserving worship like the idol worshippers.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage clarifies the Mu'tazilite position on human actions. It states that despite their views, Mu'tazila are still considered among the sects of Islam. They do not claim that the servant is an independent creator; rather, the servant creates through the means and instruments that God creates in him. They are not guilty of shirk like the Magians (who believe in two creators) or like idol worshippers. This is a defensive clarification, possibly in response to charges of kufr against the Mu'tazila.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الخلق
الإشراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any claim of human creation implies shirk.
Correction: This passage shows that Mu'tazila avoid shirk by situating human creation as dependent on God's provision of means.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the Mu'tazilite view on the servant's creation of actions?
A) The servant is an independent creator.
B) The servant creates through the means and instruments that God created in him.
C) The servant does not create at all.
D) The servant and God both create the same action independently.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila are considered Muslims because their view of human creation does not challenge God's unique sovereignty, as it is mediated through God-given means.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been quick to label a fellow Muslim as misguided based on a theological view you didn't fully understand? Will you now pause and learn their actual position before judging?
?

The Best of People After the Prophet and the Case of Jesus
The author starts the discussion on the superiority of the Companions and mentions Jesus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate about who is the best of people after the Prophet and the special case of Jesus in relation to temporal precedence.

Core Idea
Jesus existed before the Prophet but will descend after him – a special case in the discussion of superiority.

The author begins a discussion on the superiority of the Companions, but first clarifies what 'after the Messenger' means. He addresses a potential confusion regarding Jesus, who existed before the Prophet yet will return after him. This lesson explores that distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the basic meaning.
Notice that the passage cuts off mid-sentence.
2
Understand 'after his existence'
The phrase 'after the Messenger' is explained as 'after his existence' because he is the seal of the prophets during his time. Consider what this means for ranking.
This sets the temporal framework for the discussion.
3
Examine the case of Jesus
The author mentions that Jesus existed before the Prophet but his descent will occur after. Why is this special? It means Jesus is not included in the category of those who are 'after' in the same way.
4
Consider the implication for Companions
The passage introduces the topic of preference among the Companions. Think about how the clarification about Jesus sets the stage for ranking the Companions – likely that Abu Bakr is best after the Prophet.
This connects to standard Sunni belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المفاضلة بين الصحابة
وأفضل الناس بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أي بعد وجوده لأنه خاتم النبيين حال شهوده وأما عيسى فقد وجد قبله وإن كان يقع نزوله بعده، ولا يبعد أن يقال: اد الإمام الأعظم البعدية الزمانية؛ ففي شرح المقاصد: ذهب العظماء العلماء إلى أن
The preference among the Companions. And the best of people after the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, meaning after his existence, because he is the seal of the prophets during his time. As for Jesus, he existed before him, although his descent will occur after him. It is not unlikely to say: Imam al-A'zam said temporal posteriority; for in Sharh al-Maqasid: the great scholars went to that
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of the superiority of the Companions (al-Mufadalah bayn al-Sahabah). The author states that the best of people after the Messenger of Allah means after his existence in this world, because he is the seal of the prophets during the time he was present. A special case is mentioned: Jesus ('Isa), who existed before the Prophet but will descend after him. The author then references Imam Abu Hanifa (Imam al-A'zam) regarding temporal posteriority and cites Sharh al-Maqasid. The text is incomplete, but it indicates the beginning of a discussion on who is the best after the Prophet, likely leading to the standard Sunni view that Abu Bakr is the best of the Companions.

الصحابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Jesus is among those who are considered 'after the Prophet' because he will descend after him, making him potentially superior to the Companions.
Correction: The passage clarifies that Jesus existed before the Prophet, so he is not included in the category of 'after his existence'. The discussion about the best after the Prophet pertains to those who came after his existence, not to prophets who lived before.

Checkpoint
What is the special case mentioned regarding 'the best of people after the Messenger'?
A) No one is best except the Prophet.
B) Jesus existed before the Prophet but will descend after him.
C) All prophets are equal.
D) The best is not specified.

Key Takeaway
The passage clarifies that 'after the Messenger' refers to those who came after his earthly existence, and Jesus is a unique case who existed before but will descend after.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Jesus existed before the Prophet but will descend after him. Does this uniqueness unsettle your understanding of prophethood and hierarchy? Do you hold any hidden assumption that chronological order determines spiritual rank?
?
When you say 'after the Prophet', do you truly consider the Companions and their sacrifices, or do you sometimes elevate later figures without realizing they come after the Companions? How can you cultivate a heart that respects the correct order set by the scholars?

The Prophet's Multiple Indications for Abu Bakr's Caliphate
Sanusi responds to a doubt and presents the argument from multiple indications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Prophet's multiple indications pointed to Abu Bakr as his successor, despite not leaving a written will.

Core Idea
The Prophet left no written will because his multiple signs were clear enough.

The author addresses a doubt concerning the Prophet's statements during his final illness. Some questioned whether these words were reliable, but the author argues that the Prophet refrained from writing a will precisely because his prior indications were sufficient. This lesson examines how multiple signs led the Companions to recognize Abu Bakr as the rightful caliph.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the doubt
Begin by reading the Arabic passage that addresses the question of whether the Prophet's statements during illness were binding. Notice how the author dismisses the doubt by citing the Prophet's omission of writing.
The phrase 'ترك الكتابة اكتفاء بما سبق' is key.
2
Analyze the argument from ambiguity
The author argues that if the designation were ambiguous, the Prophet would have clarified it definitively. Understand the logical structure: if there was ambiguity, he would have removed it; since he did not, there was no ambiguity.
3
Identify the multiple indications
The passage states that the Prophet gave multiple indications (دلالات متعددة) that Abu Bakr is the designated one. Reflect on what these might have been, based on the context of Fiqh al-Akbar.
The text does not enumerate them, but they are implied.
4
Connect to the conclusion
The Companions understood these indications and the objective was achieved. Note that the community recognized Abu Bakr's caliphate unanimously.
This sets up the second lesson on the allegiance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح الفقه الأكبر ثم عمر بن الخطاب صور لبعضهم شك، هل ذلك القول من جهة المرض أو هو قول يجب اتباعه؟ ترك الكتابة اكتفاء بما سبق، فلو كان التعيين مما يشتبه على الأمة لبينه بيانا قاطعا للمعذرة، لكن لما دلهم دلالات متعددة على أن أبا بكر هو المتعين وفهموا ذلك حصل المقصود هنالك. ثم الأنصار كلهم بايعوا أبا بكر إلا سعد بن عبادة لكونه هو الذي كان يطلب الولاية لنفسه، ولذا لما بايع عمر وأبو عبيدة ومن حضر من الأنصار قال قائل: قتلتم سعدا، فقال عمر: قتله الله، ولم يقل أحد من الصحابة رضي الله عنهم إن النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم نص على غير أبي بكر رضي الله عنه من علي وعباس وغيرهما رضي الله عنهم، ولو كان لأظهره. وروى ابن بطة بإسناده أن عمر بن عبد العزيز بعث محمد بن زبير الحنظلي إلى الحسن البصري فقال: هل كان النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم استخلف أبا بكر؟. فقال: أو في شك صاحبك ! نعم والله الذي لا إله إلا هو استخلفه له وكان أتقى الله من أن يتوثب عليها.
Commentary on Fiqh al-Akbar. Then Umar ibn al-Khattab – an image for some of them: doubt, whether that statement is from the aspect of illness or is it a statement that must be followed? He omitted writing, content with what preceded. For if the designation were something that would be ambiguous to the community, he would have clarified it with a definitive explanation cutting off excuse. But when he gave them multiple indications that Abu Bakr is the designated one, and they understood that, the objective was achieved there. Then all the Ansar gave allegiance to Abu Bakr except Sa'd ibn Ubadah, because he was the one who was seeking leadership for himself. Hence, when Umar, Abu Ubayda, and those present from the Ansar gave allegiance, a speaker said: 'You have killed Sa'd.' So Umar said: 'May Allah kill him.' And none of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, said that the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, explicitly designated someone other than Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, among Ali, Abbas, and others, may Allah be pleased with them; and if he had, he would have made it manifest. Ibn Battah related with his chain that Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz sent Muhammad ibn Zubayr al-Hanzali to al-Hasan al-Basri and said: 'Did the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, appoint Abu Bakr as caliph?' He replied: 'Is your companion in doubt? Yes, by Allah besides whom there is no god, he appointed him as caliph, and he was more God-fearing than to leap upon it.'
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the caliphate of Abu Bakr and answers objections. The author begins by addressing a doubt: some questioned whether the Prophet's statements during his illness were from illness or binding. The author argues that the Prophet refrained from writing a will because the prior indications were sufficient. If the designation had been ambiguous, the Prophet would have clarified it definitively, but he gave multiple signs pointing to Abu Bakr, and the Companions understood. Then the author recounts the allegiance of the Ansar, except Sa'd ibn Ubadah who wanted the position for himself. The incident where Umar responded harshly to a comment shows the strength of consensus. The author notes that no Companion ever claimed the Prophet designated another, and if he had, it would have been publicized. Finally, a report from al-Hasan al-Basri confirms that the Prophet did appoint Abu Bakr as caliph, emphasizing that Abu Bakr was too God-fearing to seize power.

Key Terms
أبو بكر
التعيين
الدلالات
المعذرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Prophet did not designate a successor because he did not write a will.
Correction: The Prophet gave multiple verbal and situational indications that were clear to the Companions, making a written will unnecessary.

Checkpoint
What did the author rely on to prove that Abu Bakr was designated as caliph, instead of a written will?
A) A direct revelation
B) Multiple indications given by the Prophet
C) The consensus of the Muhajirun only
D) The nomination by Umar

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's multiple indications were sufficient to designate Abu Bakr without a written will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face uncertainty in matters of faith, do you seek clear signs from Allah or demand written guarantees? How does the Prophet's trust in multiple indications challenge your own need for explicit proof?
?
The Companions understood the Prophet's indications without a written will. Do you trust that Allah's guidance is clear enough for you to recognize without constant reassurance? What obscures your understanding?
?
If you were in the position of the early Muslims, would you have accepted the multiple indications for Abu Bakr's caliphate, or would you have demanded a written text? What does your answer reveal about your reliance on outward forms versus inner conviction?

The Consensus of the Companions on Abu Bakr's Caliphate
Sanusi cites historical consensus and a report from Hasan al-Basri.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the allegiance of the Ansar and the testimony of Hasan al-Basri confirm that Abu Bakr was the rightful caliph.

Core Idea
The Companions gave allegiance to Abu Bakr, and no one claimed the Prophet designated another.

After establishing that the Prophet gave multiple indications, the author now cites the historical event of the allegiance given to Abu Bakr by all the Ansar except Sa'd ibn Ubadah, and he explains Sa'd's motive. He further strengthens the argument by noting that no Companion ever claimed the Prophet designated someone else, and he relates a report from Hasan al-Basri affirming the appointment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the allegiance
Read the Arabic passage describing how the Ansar all gave allegiance to Abu Bakr except Sa'd ibn Ubadah, who sought leadership for himself.
Note the reaction of Umar when it was said 'You have killed Sa'd.'
2
Understand the argument from silence
The author points out that none of the Companions claimed that the Prophet designated Ali or Abbas instead. This silence supports that no such designation existed.
The phrase 'ولو كان لأظهره' implies that if there were such a claim, it would have been public.
3
Analyze the report from Hasan al-Basri
Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz sent a question to Hasan al-Basri about whether the Prophet appointed Abu Bakr. Hasan confirms emphatically, adding that Abu Bakr was too God-fearing to seize the caliphate.
This report strengthens the Sunnite position.

The Strongest Proof of Abu Bakr's Superiority
Author states the strongest evidence for Abu Bakr's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Prophet's appointment of Abu Bakr to lead prayer during his final illness is considered the strongest evidence for Abu Bakr's superiority.

Core Idea
Prophetic appointment to lead prayer reveals divinely chosen rank.

In theological discussions about the status of the Companions, Abu Bakr's superiority is a key point. The author presents the most compelling evidence from the Prophet's own action. Let's examine this concise but powerful argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author presents a single, concrete piece of evidence.
2
Identify the evidence
What specific action of the Prophet is cited? It is the appointment to lead the congregational prayer during his illness.
This is a public, repeated action over days and nights.
3
Understand the implication
Why does leading prayer imply superiority? In Islamic tradition, leading prayer is a position of honor and authority, and the Prophet would only appoint the best person.
Consider the context: the Prophet was too ill to lead himself.
4
Reflect on alternative views
Think about possible objections: Could it be due to other reasons? The author's point is that this is the 'best evidence' because it is direct and unambiguous.
Other evidences might be circumstantial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأولى ما يستدل به على أفضلية الصديق في مقام التحقيق نصبه عليه الصلاة والسلام لإمامة الأنام مدة مرضه في الليالي والأيام،
And the best evidence to indicate the superiority of al-Siddiq (Abu Bakr) in the position of verification is his appointment, peace be upon him, to lead the people in prayer during his illness, night and day.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a concise argument for the superiority of Abu Bakr over other Companions. The author asserts that the most compelling evidence is that the Prophet, during his final illness, appointed Abu Bakr to lead the congregational prayers. This act, performed consistently over days and nights, demonstrates that the Prophet considered Abu Bakr the most suitable to lead in religious matters, implying his superior rank.

Key Terms
أفضلية الصديق
إمامة الأنام
نصبه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that superiority is determined by worldly achievements or personal virtues like early conversion or wealth.
Correction: The author points to a concrete prophetic action—appointment to lead prayer—as the strongest indicator, implying that divine selection through the Prophet's practice is more authoritative.

Checkpoint
What does the author consider the best evidence for Abu Bakr's superiority?
A) His early conversion to Islam
B) His wealth and lineage
C) The Prophet appointing him to lead prayer during illness
D) The consensus of the Companions

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's appointment of Abu Bakr to lead prayer during his final illness is the strongest proof of his superiority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet ﷺ chose Abu Bakr to lead the prayer during his final illness, a trust that Abu Bakr upheld. Consider a responsibility entrusted to you by Allah or His messenger. Have you been faithful to it, or have you neglected it? What will you change today?
?
This lesson shows that the strongest evidence of superiority comes from prophetic approval, not worldly achievements. By what standard do you measure your worth? Are you seeking Allah's approval more than people's praise? What specific step can you take to realign your priorities?
?
The Prophet's choice was continuous—night and day. Is your obedience to Allah consistent, or is it limited to certain times? Think of one area where you have been inconsistent. What will you do to make your commitment unwavering like Abu Bakr's appointment?

Order of Preference Among the Four Caliphs
Discusses the Ahl al-Sunnah view on the order of superiority and refutes opposing views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Sunnah position on the superiority of the four caliphs, including the consensus on Abu Bakr and Umar, and the nuanced views on Uthman and Ali.

Core Idea
The order of the caliphs reflects both scholarly consensus and careful nuance.

This lesson examines the Ahl al-Sunnah view on the relative merits of the first four caliphs. While the superiority of Abu Bakr and Umar is agreed upon, the ranking of Uthman and Ali has been a point of discussion. The passage from our text clarifies the mainstream position and addresses contrary opinions from various groups.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice the structure: first a consensus statement, then disagreements, then a nuanced conclusion.
The Arabic ends abruptly, but the main argument is complete.
2
Identify the key opinions
List what each group (Ahl al-Sunnah, Rawafid, Mu'tazila, and the report from Abu Hanifa) holds regarding the order of the caliphs.
Pay attention to the distinction between the first two caliphs and the latter two.
3
Understand the nuance
Focus on the final distinction: superiority by reward vs. by worldly virtues. Why does the author say there is hesitation in one case but not the other?
This nuance is key to the Ahl al-Sunnah position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتفضيل أبي بكر وعمر رضي الله عنهما متفق عليه بين أهل السنة وهذا الترتيب بين عثمان وعلي رضي الله عنهما هو ما عليه أكثر أهل السنة، خلافا لما روي عن بعض أهل الكوفة والبصرة من عكس القضية. ثم اعلم جميع الروافض وأكثر المعتزلة يفضلون عليا على أبي بكر رضي عنه، وروي عن أبي حنيفة رضي الله عنه تفضيل علي رضي الله عنه على عثمان رضي الله عنه، والصحيح ما عليه جمهور أهل السنة وهو الظاهر من قول أبي حنيفة رضي الله عنه على ما رتبه هنا وفق مراتب الخلافة. وفي 'شرح العقائد على هذا الترتيب وجدنا السلف والظاهر أنه لو لم يكن لهم دليل هنالك لما حكموا بذلك، وكأن السلف كانوا متوقفين في تفضيل عثمان رضي الله عنه على علي رضي الله عنه حيث جعلوا من علامات السنة والجماعة تفضيل الشيخين ومحبة الختنين. والإنصاف أنه إن أريد بالأفضلية كثرة الثواب فللتوقف جهة وإن أريد كثرة ما يعده ذوو العقول من الفضائل فلا، انتهى. وماده بالأفضلية أفضلية عثمان على علي رضي الله عنه بقرينة ما قبله من ذكر التوقف فيما بينهما، لا الأفضلية بين الأربعة كما فهم أكثر المحسين حيث قال بعضهم بعد قوله فلا لأن
And the preference of Abu Bakr and Umar (may Allah be pleased with them) is agreed upon among Ahl al-Sunnah, and this order between Uthman and Ali (may Allah be pleased with them) is what most Ahl al-Sunnah adhere to, contrary to what is narrated from some of the people of Kufa and Basra who reverse the case. Then know that all the Rawafid and most of the Mu'tazila prefer Ali over Abu Bakr (may Allah be pleased with him). And it is narrated from Abu Hanifa (may Allah be pleased with him) that he preferred Ali over Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him). The correct view is that of the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah, which is apparent from the statement of Abu Hanifa (may Allah be pleased with him) according to the order he arranged here following the order of the caliphate. In 'Sharh al-Aqa'id': 'Upon this order we found the Salaf, and it is apparent that if they had no evidence there, they would not have ruled accordingly. It is as if the Salaf were hesitant regarding the preference of Uthman over Ali, since they made the preference of the two Shaykhs (Abu Bakr and Umar) and love of the two sons-in-law (Uthman and Ali) among the signs of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah. The fair assessment is that if by superiority is meant abundance of reward, then there is room for hesitation; but if it means abundance of what people of intellect count as virtues, then no hesitation.' End. And his meaning by superiority is the superiority of Uthman over Ali, by the context of the preceding mention of hesitation between them, not the superiority among the four, as most of the commentators understood, where some of them said after his statement 'then no' because...
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal classification and refutation regarding the order of preference among the first four caliphs. It begins by stating that the superiority of Abu Bakr and Umar over the other two is unanimously agreed upon by Ahl al-Sunnah. The order between Uthman and Ali is that Uthman is preferred by the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah, contrary to a reported reversal by some people of Kufa and Basra. Then it notes that the Rawafid and most Mu'tazila prefer Ali over Abu Bakr, and that Abu Hanifa reportedly preferred Ali over Uthman. The correct position according to the author is the majority view of Ahl al-Sunnah, which is also the apparent meaning of Abu Hanifa's statement when interpreted in light of his arrangement according to the order of the caliphate. A quote from 'Sharh al-Aqa'id' explains that the Salaf may have been hesitant about preferring Uthman over Ali because they made the signs of Ahl al-Sunnah merely the preference of the two Shaykhs (Abu Bakr and Umar) and love of the two sons-in-law (Uthman and Ali), without explicitly ordering the latter pair. The fair conclusion is that if superiority is measured by abundant reward, there is hesitation; but if by worldly virtues, then Uthman is clearly superior. The passage ends by clarifying that the author's discussion is specifically about the preference between Uthman and Ali, not the entire four, although some commentators misunderstood this. The text cuts off mid-sentence.

السلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Ahl al-Sunnah unanimously agree on the exact order among all four caliphs without any nuance.
Correction: The passage shows that while Abu Bakr and Umar are clearly superior, the order between Uthman and Ali is nuanced: Uthman is superior in worldly virtues, but reward-based superiority is uncertain.

Checkpoint
What is the correct view on the order of preference between Uthman and Ali according to the passage?
A) Ali is definitely superior to Uthman
B) Uthman is definitely superior to Ali
C) There is no preference; they are equal
D) Uthman is superior based on worldly virtues, but reward-based superiority is uncertain

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunnah holds that Abu Bakr and Umar are superior to Uthman and Ali, but the order between Uthman and Ali is nuanced, with Uthman preferred in worldly virtues while reward-based superiority remains uncertain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of the Companions, do you catch yourself preferring one over another based on tribal or sectarian bias? This lesson teaches that the Salaf themselves were cautious. Will you commit to studying the virtues of all the four caliphs without prejudice? Make a concrete plan to learn their stories without bias.
?
The passage distinguishes between superiority in worldly virtues and superiority in reward. Which standard do you use when evaluating people in your community? Are you dazzled by worldly achievements while neglecting spiritual merit? Pledge today to recalibrate your measure of a person's worth based on taqwa, not status.
?
The Salaf hesitated rather than rushing to a definitive ranking. In what area of your religious knowledge do you need to admit uncertainty instead of pretending to know? Identify one matter where you will say 'Allahu a'lam' and leave it to the scholars. This is the humility of Ahl al-Sunnah.

The Sunni Consensus on the Order of the Caliphs
Author states Sunni consensus on caliphal order and addresses differing views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni consensus on the preference and order of the Rightly Guided Caliphs, as well as the differing views and the nuance regarding the order of Uthman and Ali.

Core Idea
The consensus of Ahl al-Sunnah is Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman, then Ali.

This passage from Manah al-Rawd al-Azhar establishes the Sunni position on the ranking of the Rightly Guided Caliphs. The author cites the consensus of the Companions and the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah, while acknowledging and addressing contrasting views from the Rawafid, Mu'tazila, and even a narration from Abu Hanifa. The passage also provides a nuanced discussion on what 'superiority' means, particularly regarding Uthman and Ali.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basis for appointing the best as caliph
Read the opening part where the author quotes the senior Companions and the rule from al-Khulasah about choosing the best for leadership.
This establishes the principle that the caliph must be the best among the community.
2
Identify the Sunni consensus order and contrasting views
Locate the statement of consensus on Abu Bakr and Umar, then the order of Uthman and Ali. Note the opposing views from some people of Kufa and Basra, the Rawafid, Mu'tazila, and the narration from Abu Hanifa.
The majority view is clear, but there are minority opinions.
3
Examine the nuance about Uthman and Ali
Read the final part from Sharh al-Aqa'id where the Salaf's hesitation is mentioned and the distinction between superiority in reward vs. virtue.
This clarifies that the order is about virtue, not necessarily reward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولذا قال أكابر الصحابة رضي الله تعالى عنه: «لديننا أفلا نرضاه لدنيانا؟» ثم إجماع جمهورهم على نصبه للخلافة ومتابعة غيرهم أيضا في آخر أمرهم. ففي "الخلاصة": رجلان في الفقه والصلاح سواء إلا أن أحدهما أقرأ فقدم أهل المسجد الآخر فقد أساؤوا، وكذا لو قلد القضاء رجلا وهو من أهله وغيره أفضل منه، وكذا الوالي. وأما الخليفة فليس لهم أن يولوا الخلافة إلا أفضلهم، وهذا في الخلفاء خاصة وعليه إجماع الأمة. انتهى. وتفضيل أبي بكر وعمر رضي الله عنهما متفق عليه بين أهل السنة، وهذا الترتيب بين عثمان وعلي رضي الله عنهما هو ما عليه أكثر أهل السنة، خلافا لما روي عن بعض أهل الكوفة والبصرة من عكس القضية. ثم اعلم أن جميع الروافض وأكثر المعتزلة يفضلون عليا على أبي بكر رضي الله عنه، وروي عن أبي حنيفة رضي الله عنه تفضيل علي رضي الله عنه على عثمان رضي الله عنه، والصحيح ما عليه جمهور أهل السنة وهو الظاهر من قول أبي حنيفة رضي الله عنه على ما رتبه هنا وفق مراتب الخلافة. وفي "شرح العقائد": على هذا الترتيب وجدنا السلف، والظاهر أنه لو لم يكن لهم دليل هنالك لما حكموا بذلك، وكأن السلف كانوا متوقفين في تفضيل عثمان رضي الله عنه على علي رضي الله عنه حيث جعلوا من علامات السنة والجماعة تفضيل الشيخين ومحبة الختنين. والإنصاف أنه إن أريد بالأفضلية كثرة الثواب فللتوقف جهة، وإن أريد كثرة ما يعده ذوو العقول من الفضائل فلا. انتهى.
Therefore the senior Companions said, may Allah be pleased with him: 'For our religion, are we not pleased with him for our worldly life?' Then the consensus of the majority of them upon his appointment to the caliphate and the following of others also in the end of their affair. For in 'al-Khulasah': Two men equal in jurisprudence and righteousness except that one of them is more learned in recitation, so the people of the mosque put forward the other, they are wrong. Likewise if he appoints a man to judgeship while there is someone better from his people, and another better than him, and likewise the governor. As for the caliph, they may not appoint anyone to the caliphate except their best, and this is specific to the caliphs, and upon it is the consensus of the community. End. And the preference of Abu Bakr and Umar, may Allah be pleased with them, is agreed upon among Ahl al-Sunnah, and this arrangement between Uthman and Ali, may Allah be pleased with them, is what most of Ahl al-Sunnah are upon, contrary to what is narrated from some of the people of Kufa and Basra of reversing the matter. Then know that all of the Rawafid and most of the Mu'tazila prefer Ali over Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, and it is narrated from Abu Hanifa, may Allah be pleased with him, the preference of Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, over Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him. And the correct view is that of the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah, and it is apparent from the statement of Abu Hanifa, may Allah be pleased with him, based on what he arranged here according to the ranks of the caliphate. In 'Sharh al-Aqa'id': Upon this arrangement we found the Salaf, and apparently if they had no proof for that, they would not have judged so. It is as if the Salaf were hesitant regarding preferring Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, over Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, since they made from the signs of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah the preference of the two shaykhs and love of the two sons-in-law. And fairness is that if by 'superiority' is meant abundance of reward, there is room for hesitation, but if what is intended is abundance of what people of intellect count as virtues, then no. End.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage discusses the Sunni consensus on the preference (tafdil) of the Rightly Guided Caliphs. It begins by citing a statement from the senior Companions regarding the appointment of the best person for the caliphate, reinforcing that the community agreed to appoint only the best. The author then quotes 'al-Khulasah' to show that in leadership positions like judges and governors, the best should be chosen, and this applies especially to the caliph. He then states the well-known Sunni position: the preference of Abu Bakr and Umar is agreed upon, and the order of Uthman then Ali is the position of most Ahl al-Sunnah, contrary to some from Kufa and Basra who reverse Uthman and Ali. He notes that the Rawafid and most Mu'tazila prefer Ali over Abu Bakr, and that a narration from Abu Hanifa prefers Ali over Uthman, but the correct view is the majority view, which is apparent from Abu Hanifa's own arrangement. The author then quotes 'Sharh al-Aqa'id' to show that the Salaf were found upon this order, and that they might have been hesitant about preferring Uthman over Ali, because they made signs of Sunnism include preferring the two shaykhs (Abu Bakr and Umar) and loving the two sons-in-law (Uthman and Ali). The passage concludes with a nuance: if 'superiority' means more reward, there is room for hesitation; but if it means more virtues by rational estimation, then there is no hesitation (i.e., Uthman is superior). This establishes the Sunni position while acknowledging some historical nuance.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The order of the caliphs is disputed among all Sunnis.
Correction: The order is agreed upon by the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah, with only minor differences.

Checkpoint

A) Only Abu Bakr and Umar are preferred, with Uthman and Ali equal.
B) Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman, then Ali.
C) Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Ali, then Uthman.
D) Ali is preferred over all others.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni consensus orders the Rightly Guided Caliphs as Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and Ali, with nuances about the meaning of superiority between Uthman and Ali.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a consensus of scholars on a matter (like the caliphal order), do you humbly accept it or internally resist? What in your heart needs to submit to the collective wisdom of the Salaf?
?
The passage shows that even among early Muslims there was some hesitation in preferring one Companion over another. Are you ever tempted to elevate personal opinions or sectarian loyalties over the established consensus? How can you guard against that?
?
The Salaf made love of both Uthman and Ali a sign of the true Sunnah. Do your own heart and speech reflect that balanced love for all the Companions, or do you find yourself leaning toward one at the expense of the other? What step will you take today to correct that?
