Consensus of Ahl al-Sunna on Abu Bakr's Superiority
The author clarifies the nature of the consensus on Abu Bakr's superiority and supports it with a hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the consensus (ijma') on Abu Bakr's superiority is the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna, not the entire umma, and the evidence from the hadith of Sa'id ibn Zayd.

Core Idea
The consensus on Abu Bakr’s superiority is the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna, not the entire umma.

This lesson clarifies a frequent point of discussion: the alleged consensus on the superiority of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq. The author explains that this consensus is not absolute but is specifically the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna, since some innovators hold differing views. He then supports this with a powerful hadith from one of the ten promised Paradise, Sa'id ibn Zayd, highlighting the immense virtue of the early companions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Observe the author's concise style and the flow from conclusion to evidence.
The passage is short but dense; read it twice to absorb the distinction being made.
2
Understand the author's key distinction
Notice how the author restricts the consensus to 'those who are considered among Ahl al-Sunna' and explains why it cannot be attributed to the entire umma due to opposition from Ahl al-Bid'a.
This distinction is crucial for correctly understanding the scope of ijma' on this issue.
3
Reflect on the hadith evidence
Consider the hadith of Sa'id ibn Zayd. It emphasizes the superiority of being among the ten companions (including Abu Bakr) over a lifetime of deeds. Reflect on how this supports the consensus claim.
The hadith is authentic and serves as a textual proof for the virtue of the ten.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أن ما ذكره بعضهم من الإجماع على أفضلية الصديق محمول على إجماع من يُعتد به من أهل السنة؛ إذ لا يصح حمله على إجماع الأمة لمخالفة بعض أهل البدعة، وقد قال سعيد بن زيد: «المشهد رجل من العشرة مع رسول الله صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم يغبر منه وجهه خير من عمل أحدكم ولو عُمّر عمر نوح». رواه أبو داود وابن ماجه والترمذي وصححه.
The conclusion is that what some have mentioned concerning the consensus (ijma') on the superiority (afdaliyya) of al-Siddiq (Abu Bakr) is carried upon the consensus of those who are considered among Ahl al-Sunna; for it is not correct to carry it upon the consensus of the entire umma due to the opposition of some from Ahl al-Bid'a (people of innovation). And Sa'id ibn Zayd said: 'The witnessing of a man among the ten with the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, whose face becomes dusty from it, is better than the deeds of any one of you even if he lived the age of Noah.' Reported by Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah, and al-Tirmidhi, and he declared it authentic.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage establishes the consensus regarding the superiority of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq. The author clarifies that this consensus is not claimed for the entire Muslim community because the innovators oppose it. Rather, it is the consensus of those counted as Ahl al-Sunna. To support this, he cites the hadith of Sa'id ibn Zayd, who was one of the ten companions promised Paradise. The hadith states that being among the ten companions with the Prophet, even in a humble condition, is better than the deeds of anyone else even if they lived as long as Noah. This hadith underscores the immense virtue of the ten, especially Abu Bakr.

Key Terms

أهل السنة
أفضلية الصديق
أهل البدعة
سعيد بن زيد
العشرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the consensus on Abu Bakr's superiority is accepted by all Muslims without exception.
Correction: The author clarifies that the consensus is only from Ahl al-Sunna; innovators (Ahl al-Bid'a) oppose it, so it is not a consensus of the entire umma.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The superiority of Abu Bakr is agreed upon by Ahl al-Sunna, not by those who oppose the Sunna.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The hadith teaches that a moment with the Prophet is greater than a lifetime of deeds—does your daily life reflect that you value the companionship of the Prophet and his followers above all worldly achievements?

Refuting the Hatred of the Word 'Ten'
Refutation of number superstition with scriptural proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the irrationality of hating a number due to its association with certain people, based on Quranic and prophetic evidence.

Core Idea
Hating a number because of people is both illogical and contradicts the Quran and Sunnah.

The author addresses a peculiar sectarian prejudice: avoiding the word 'ten' because of the ten companions promised Paradise. He exposes the inconsistency of this attitude and provides strong textual evidence that the number ten is used positively in the Quran and hadith. This lesson will help you see why such prejudices are rationally and religiously baseless.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation to grasp the argument.
Notice the emotional tone—it's a refutation, not a neutral explanation.
2
Identify the main argument
The author argues that hating the word 'ten' is ignorance. Find the key irony: they use 'nine' while hating nine of the ten companions.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
List the Quranic verses and hadiths the author uses to show that 'ten' occurs in positive contexts.
Note that the author does not deny the status of the ten companions; rather, he defends the word itself.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: what would be a more rational approach to the number ten? The author's point is that numbers are neutral; hate should be directed at beliefs, not digits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمن أجهل ممن يكره التكلم بلفظ العشرة أو فعل شيء يكون عشرة لكونهم يبغضون خيار الصحابة وهم العشرة المشهود لهم بالجنة، وهم يستثنون منهم ومن العجب أنهم يولون لفظ التسعة وهم يبغضون التسعة من العشرة، ويبغضون سائر الصحابة من المهاجرين والأنصار الذين قال الله تعالى في حقهم : رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُمْ وَرَضُوا عَنْهُ [البيئة: ۸] ، إلا عن نفر قليل نحو بضعة عشر نفرا، ومعلوم أنه لو فرض في العالم عشرة من أكفر الناس لم يجب هجر هذا الاسم لذلك، كما أنه سبحانه لما قال : وَكَانَ فِي الْمَدِينَةِ يَسْعَةُ رَهْطٍ يُفْسِدُونَ فِي الْأَرْضِ وَلَا يُصْلِحُونَ ﴾ [النمل:٤٨] ، لم يجب هجر اسم التسعة مطلقاً بل اسم العشرة الله تعالى مسماه في مواضع من القرآن كقوله تعالى: ﴿ تِلْكَ عَشَرَةٌ كَامِلَةٌ﴾ [البقرة: ١٩٦]، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَأَتْمَمْتُهَا بِعَشْرِ﴾ [الأعراف : ١٤٢]، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَالْفَجْرِ وَلَيَالٍ عَشْرِ [الفجر: ١-٢]، وكان صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم يعتكف العشر الأول من رمضان، وقال في ليلة القدر: ((التمسوها في العشر الأواخر)) (۱) وقال : ((ما) من أيام العمل الصالح فيهن أحب إلى الله من أيام العشر)) . (٢) يعني عشر ذي الحجة.
Who is more ignorant than the one who dislikes speaking the word 'ten' or doing something that is ten, because they hate the best of the Companions, who are the ten who were given glad tidings of Paradise. And they exclude some of them. And it is amazing that they adopt the word 'nine' while they hate the nine among the ten, and they hate the rest of the Companions from the Muhajirun and Ansar concerning whom God Almighty said: 'God is well pleased with them and they are well pleased with Him' [Surah al-Bayyinah? 98:8] except for a small number of about a dozen people. It is well known that if there were in the world ten of the most disbelieving people, it would not be necessary to abandon this word because of that. Likewise, when He, glorified, said: 'And there were in the city nine persons who caused corruption in the land and would not reform' [Surah al-Naml 27:48], it was not necessary to absolutely abandon the word 'nine'. Rather, the word 'ten' is mentioned by God in places in the Qur'an, such as His saying: 'That is ten complete' [Surah al-Baqarah 2:196], and His saying: 'And I completed them with ten' [Surah al-A'raf 7:142], and His saying: 'And the dawn; and ten nights' [Surah al-Fajr 89:1-2]. And the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, used to observe i'tikaf during the first ten days of Ramadan, and he said concerning the Night of Power: 'Seek it in the last ten [nights]', and he said: 'There are no days in which righteous deeds are more beloved to God than these ten days', meaning the ten days of Dhul-Hijjah.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
In this passage, the author refutes the extreme attitude of those (the Rafidah) who hate the word 'ten' because it reminds them of the ten companions promised Paradise. He points out the irony that they adopt the word 'nine' while hating nine of the ten. He argues that if there were ten disbelievers, that would not justify abandoning the word. Furthermore, the word 'ten' and 'nine' are used in the Quran in positive contexts and in the Sunnah. He cites several Quranic verses and hadiths where the number ten is mentioned positively, such as the ten days of Dhul-Hijjah and the last ten nights of Ramadan. The conclusion is that hating the word is an irrational prejudice.

Key Terms

التسعة
العشرة المبشرة
القرآن
الحديث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That it is acceptable to avoid the word 'ten' because it reminds one of the ten companions (some of whom the Rafidah dislike).
Correction: The author shows this is irrational: numbers are neutral, and the Quran and hadith use 'ten' in praiseworthy contexts.

Checkpoint
Which of the following does the author use to show the word 'ten' is not to be avoided?
A) The word 'ten' appears in the Quran only in negative contexts

Key Takeaway
Numbers themselves are neutral; hating a number because of people is irrational and contradicts the Quran and Sunnah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Make one concrete commitment today to use language exactly as the Prophet did, even when it challenges your emotions.

The Twelve Caliphs: Refuting the Rafidah's Claim
Refutes Rafidah by citing hadith and listing historical caliphs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith of the twelve caliphs refutes the Rafidah's belief in twelve imams.

Core Idea
The Prophet's twelve caliphs were historical rulers, not hidden imams.

The Rafidah (Shi'a) assert that the Prophet designated twelve imams from his family. However, authentic hadiths speak of twelve caliphs from Quraysh. This passage from the Sharh shows that the historical fulfillment of this prophecy directly contradicts the Rafidah claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its literal English translation. Notice the author's method of refutation.
Pay attention to the hadith citation and the list of caliphs.
2
Identify the twelve caliphs listed
The author enumerates specific historical figures. Write them down as you read.
The list includes the four Rightly Guided Caliphs, Umayyad rulers, and Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz.
3
Contrast with the Rafidah view
Consider the Rafidah position that these rulers were oppressors. The author declares their statement manifestly false.
Think about why the hadith's promise of uprightness conflicts with the Rafidah narrative.
4
Reflect on the argument
Ask yourself: How does listing the actual caliphs serve as a refutation?
The hadith predicts continuity under twelve rulers; the Rafidah claim that the umma was in darkness under them contradicts the hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: والروافض توالي بدل العشرة المبشرة بالجنة اثني عشر إماما، ولم يأت ذكر الأئمة الاثني عشر إلا على صفة تردّ قولهم وتبطله، وهو ما أخرجاه في الصحيحين عن جابر بن سمرة قال: دخلت مع أبي على النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم فسمعته يقول : لا يزال أمر الناس ماضيا ما وليهم اثنا عشر رجلا كلهم من قريش. وفي لفظ: لا يزال الأمر عزيزاً إلى اثني عشر خليفة)). وكان الأمر كما قال النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم؛ فالاثني عشر هم الخلفاء الراشدون الأربعة، ومعاوية وابنه يزيد، وعبد الملك بن مروان وأولاده الأربعة، وبينهم عمر بن عبد العزيز، ثم أخذ الأمر في الانحلال، وعند الرافضة أن أمر الأمة لم يزل في أيام هؤلاء فاسدا منغصا يتولاه الظالمون المعتدون، بل المنافقون الكافرون وأهل الحق أذل من اليهود، وقولهم ظاهر البطلان، والله المستعان.
He said: The Rafidah follow, instead of the ten given glad tidings of Paradise, twelve imams. And the mention of the twelve imams has not come except in a manner that refutes their statement and invalidates it. That is what they both (Bukhari and Muslim) recorded in the two Sahihs from Jabir ibn Samurah who said: I entered with my father upon the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, and heard him say: 'The affair of the people will continue to be upright as long as twelve men rule them, all of them from Quraysh.' In another version: 'The affair will remain honorable until twelve caliphs.' And the affair was as the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, said. So the twelve are the four Rightly Guided Caliphs, Mu'awiyah and his son Yazid, 'Abd al-Malik ibn Marwan and his four sons, and between them 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz. Then the affair began to disintegrate. According to the Rafidah, the affair of the umma throughout the days of these rulers was corrupt and troubled, governed by oppressors and aggressors, even hypocrites and disbelievers, and the people of truth were more humiliated than the Jews. Their statement is clearly false. And God is the Helper.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage refutes the Rafidah's belief in twelve imams by using the hadith of twelve caliphs from the Sahihs. The author notes that the mention of twelve imams in the hadith refutes the Rafidah because the hadith lists twelve caliphs from Quraysh, which historically are the first twelve caliphs after the Prophet. He enumerates them: the four Rightly Guided Caliphs, Mu'awiyah, Yazid, Abd al-Malik, his four sons, and between them Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz. After these, the caliphate declined. The Rafidah hold that these rulers were all oppressors, but the author declares their statement manifestly false.

Key Terms
الروافض
اثني عشر إماما
اثنا عشر خليفة
جابر بن سمرة
الصحيحين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith of twelve caliphs supports the Rafidah's twelve imams.
Correction: The hadith lists historical caliphs from Quraysh, not the hidden imams of the Rafidah.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which caliphs are included in the twelve?
A) The four Rightly Guided Caliphs only
B) The four Rightly Guided Caliphs, Mu'awiyah, Yazid, Abd al-Malik, his four sons, and Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz
C) The twelve imams of the Rafidah
D) Only the Umayyad caliphs

Key Takeaway
The hadith of twelve caliphs from Quraysh is historically fulfilled by the early caliphs, refuting the Rafidah's claim of twelve hidden imams.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you secretly wish that the leaders of Islam were the infallible, hidden figures of lore because it is easier to idealize them than to accept the flawed human caliphs that were actually sent? What does your attachment to utopian leadership say about your trust in Allah's wisdom in history?
?
When you read that the Prophet's promise of twelve caliphs was fulfilled by rulers like Yazid and the Umayyads, whom you may have been taught to despise, does your heart resist submitting to the plain words of the Prophet? If so, which will you follow: your sect's narrative or the authentic sunnah?
?
The Rafidah consider the era of these caliphs to be corrupt and the people of truth more humiliated than Jews. Have you ever felt superior to others by condemning historical Muslims? If your salvation depends on accepting all the Companions and their successors, are you prepared to let go of any grudge or disdain you hold against them?

The Origin of Rafidism from Abdullah ibn Saba
The author traces the origin of the Rafidah sect to the hypocrite Abdullah ibn Saba.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical origin of the Rafidah sect as traced to the hypocrite Abdullah ibn Saba.

Core Idea
Rafidism began as a malicious plot by a hypocrite, not a legitimate difference.

In this passage, the author delves into the origins of the Rafidah sect, a group that would later split from mainstream Sunni Islam. He attributes its foundation to a single individual, Abdullah ibn Saba, whose true intentions were to undermine Islam from within. The author also draws a parallel to Paul's corruption of Christianity, framing Rafidism as a deliberate act of heresy.

He eventually instigated the murder of the third Caliph Uthman. The text is cut off, but the point is made that Rafidism is a heretical innovation.

Refuting Rafidah via the Twelve Caliphs
Commentary refutes Rafidah claim of twelve imams via hadith of twelve caliphs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith of twelve caliphs refutes the Rafidah claim of twelve imams, and who those twelve caliphs are according to Sunni scholarship.

Core Idea
The hadith of twelve caliphs proves the Sunni position, not the Rafidah imams.

The Rafidah (Shia) claim that there are twelve divinely appointed imams, but the only authentic mention of twelve leaders in hadith is the Prophet's statement about twelve caliphs. In this passage, the commentator shows that these twelve caliphs are well-known historical figures, not the Shia imams. This refutation is important for understanding Sunni differences about leadership after the Prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow from claim to hadith to conclusion.
The Arabic uses dense scholarly style; the translation clarifies it.
2
Identify the Rafidah claim and the hadith
Find where the Rafidah claim is stated and where the Prophet's hadith is quoted. Observe how the hadith directly counters their interpretation.
The hadith mentions 'twelve caliphs' not 'twelve imams'.
3
Examine the list of caliphs
The commentator lists the twelve caliphs. Identify each one and see how they are grouped. This shows the hadith's fulfillment.
Compare this list with the Shia imams – they are completely different.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والروافض توالي بدل العشرة المبشرة بالجنة اثني عشر إماما، ولم يأت ذكر الأئمة الاثني عشر إلا على صفة تردّ قولهم وتبطله، وهو ما أخرجاه في الصحيحين عن جابر بن سمرة قال: دخلت مع أبي على النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم فسمعته يقول : لا يزال أمر الناس ماضيا ما وليهم اثنا عشر رجلا كلهم من قريش. وفي لفظ: لا يزال الأمر عزيزاً إلى اثني عشر خليفة)). وكان الأمر كما قال النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم؛ فالاثني عشر هم الخلفاء الراشدون الأربعة، ومعاوية وابنه يزيد، وعبد الملك بن مروان وأولاده الأربعة، وبينهم عمر بن عبد العزيز، ثم أخذ الأمر في الانحلال، وعند الرافضة أن أمر الأمة لم يزل في أيام هؤلاء فاسدا منغصا يتولاه الظالمون المعتدون، بل المنافقون الكافرون وأهل الحق أذل من اليهود، وقولهم ظاهر البطلان، والله المستعان .
The Rafidah substitute ten glad-tidings-of-Paradise with twelve imams, and the mention of the twelve imams has not come except in a description that refutes their statement and invalidates it, which is what al-Bukhari and Muslim narrated in the Sahihayn from Jabir ibn Samurah who said: I entered with my father to the Prophet (peace and blessings of Allah be upon him) and heard him say: The people's affair will continue as long as they are ruled by twelve men, all of them from Quraysh. And in another wording: The affair will remain strong until twelve caliphs. And the affair was as the Prophet (peace and blessings of Allah be upon him) said: the twelve are the four rightly-guided caliphs, Mu'awiyah and his son Yazid, Abdul-Malik ibn Marwan and his four sons, and between them Umar ibn Abdul-Aziz, then the affair began to disintegrate. And according to the Rafidah, the affair of the Ummah has never ceased in the days of these to be corrupt and unpleasant, ruled by oppressive transgressors, rather hypocritical disbelievers, and the people of truth are more humiliated than the Jews. And their statement is clearly false, and Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Rafidah (Shia) claim that there are twelve imams who are the rightful leaders. The commentator points out that the only mention of twelve leaders in authentic hadith is the hadith of twelve caliphs, which the Rafidah misinterpret. The commentator then specifies who those twelve caliphs are: the four rightly-guided caliphs (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali), Muawiyah and his son Yazid, Abdul-Malik ibn Marwan and his four sons, and Umar ibn Abdul-Aziz among them. He then notes that the Rafidah claim that the Ummah was in a state of corruption under these leaders is clearly false, because the hadith has been fulfilled as the Prophet said.

Misconception: The hadith of twelve caliphs refers to the twelve Shia imams.
Correction: The hadith refers to historical caliphs as listed: the four rightly-guided, Muawiyah, Yazid, Abdul-Malik and his four sons, and Umar ibn Abdul-Aziz.

Origin of the Rafidah Sect
Author traces the origin of the Rafidah sect to the hypocrite Abdullah ibn Saba.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical account of the Rafidah sect's origin as traced to the hypocrite Abdullah ibn Saba.

Core Idea
The Rafidah sect was founded by a deceitful heretic, not by sincere followers of Ali.

In this passage, the author traces the beginnings of the Rafidah sect back to a single individual, Abdullah ibn Saba. This account emphasizes that the sect's origin was rooted in deceit and aimed at undermining Islam from within, similar to the role of Paul in Christianity. Let's examine the details to see how this figure operated and how the early Muslim community responded.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing; then read the translation. Notice the key figures mentioned: Abdullah ibn Saba, Paul, and Ali.
Pay attention to the comparison with Paul.
2
Identify the founder and his methods
The passage states that Abdullah ibn Saba invented Rafidah. Look for the steps he took: he showed asceticism, enjoined good, forbade evil, instigated rebellion against Uthman, and later showed extremism regarding Ali.
3
Compare with Paul's role in Christianity
The author draws a parallel between Ibn Saba and Paul. Consider why this comparison is made and what it implies about the nature of Ibn Saba's corruption.
Paul is seen as corrupting Christianity from within.
4
Observe Ali's response
When Ali learned of Ibn Saba's extremism, he sought to kill him, and later flogged him for slandering Abu Bakr and Umar. This shows Ali's rejection of the sect.
This is important for understanding the historical stance of Ali.
5
Reflect on the lesson's implication
Consider how this account shapes the view of the Rafidah sect as a deviation with a malicious origin, not a legitimate difference of opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال وأصل الرفض إنما أحدثه منافق زنديق قصده إبطال دين الإسلام والقدح في الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام، كما ذكر ذلك العلماء الأعلام، فإن عبد الله بن سبأ لما أظهر الإسلام أراد أن يفسد دين الإسلام بمكره وخبثه، كما فعل بولس بدين النصارى، فأظهر التنسك ثم أظهر الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر حتى سعى في فتنة عثمان وقتله، ثم لما قدم على الكوفة أظهر الغلو في علي والنص عليه ليتمكن من ذلك، وبلغ ذلك عليا فطلب قتله فهرب منه إلى قرقيسا، وخبره معروف في التاريخ، وثبت عن علي رضي الله عنه أن فضله من أبي بكر وعمر جلده جلد المفتري.
Then he said: the origin of Rafidah was only invented by a hypocritical heretic whose aim was to invalidate the religion of Islam and to disparage the Messenger (peace and salutation be upon him), as the great scholars have mentioned. For indeed, Abdullah ibn Saba, when he outwardly accepted Islam, wanted to corrupt the religion of Islam through his deceit and wickedness, as Paul did to the Christian religion. So he outwardly showed asceticism, then he enjoined what is right and forbade what is wrong until he instigated the rebellion against Uthman and his murder. Then when he came to Kufa, he showed extremism regarding Ali and the textual designation (of succession) for him, so that he could accomplish his aim. This reached Ali, so he sought to kill him, but he fled from him to Qarqisa. His story is well-known in history, and it is established from Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) that he flogged him with the flogging of one who fabricates lies, due to his preference for Abu Bakr and Umar.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage explains the historical origin of the Rafidah sect. The author states that it was invented by a hypocritical heretic named Abdullah ibn Saba, who outwardly embraced Islam but intended to corrupt it, similar to how Paul corrupted Christianity. He feigned asceticism and commanded good and forbade evil, eventually instigating the rebellion against Uthman and his murder. Later, he moved to Kufa and spread extremism regarding Ali and the claim that Ali was explicitly designated as successor. When Ali learned of this, he sought to kill him, but Ibn Saba fled. The commentator adds that Ali himself flogged Ibn Saba for slandering Abu Bakr and Umar, showing Ali's respect for them.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Rafidah sect arose from sincere love for Ali and rejection of injustice.
Correction: This passage traces its origin to a hypocrite who used devotion to Ali as a cover to destroy Islam from within, and Ali himself opposed him.

Refuting Sectarian Claims about the Companions
Commentary on 'remaining on truth' refutes Rafidah and Khawarij.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Sunni creed affirms the Companions' constancy in truth and refutes the Rafidah and Khawarij.

Core Idea
The Companions remain on the truth, refuting claims of change or apostasy.

In this passage, the commentator explains the creedal statement that the Companions remain on the truth. This serves as a direct refutation of two groups: the Rafidah who allege that the first three caliphs changed after the Prophet, and the Khawarij who declare Ali and Muawiyah disbelievers. Let's read the source text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the key terms 'غابرين على الحق' and the named groups.
2
Identify the two refuted groups
The passage explicitly mentions the Rafidah and the Khawarij. Note what each group claims about the Companions.
Rafidah: the three caliphs changed. Khawarij: Ali and Muawiyah are disbelievers.
3
Understand the basis for refutation
The commentator argues that the Companions remain on the truth as they were, supported by Quranic verses and hadith. Think about why this is a strong refutation.
The evidence from revelation shows their virtue.
4
Reflect on the implications for Sunni 'aqidah
This passage reinforces the Sunni stance of loving all Companions and rejecting any doctrinal deviations about them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غابرين على الحق كما كانوا، نتولاهم جميعاً. وزيد في نسخة "ومع الحق"، أي باقين عليه، ومعه دائمين كما كانوا في الماضي من غير تغير حالهم ونقصان في كمالهم. وفيه ردّ على الروافض حيث يقولون في حق الثلاثة: إنهم تغيروا عما كانوا عليه في زمنه صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم، حيث نزل في حقهم الآيات الدالة على فضائلهم وورد فى شأنهم الأحاديث المشعرة عن حسن شمائلهم، وعلى الخوارج حيث يقولون بكفر علي ومن تابعه وكفر معاوية ومن شايعه، حيث ارتكبوا قتل المؤمن، وهو عندهم كبيرة مخرجة عن حد الإيمان.
Remaining on the truth as they were, we love them all. And in a copy it is added: 'and with the truth', i.e., remaining upon it and with it eternally as they were in the past, without any change in their state or deficiency in their perfection. And in this is a refutation of the Rafidah who say concerning the three (caliphs): that they changed from what they were upon in his time (peace and blessings of Allah be upon him), despite the revelation of verses indicating their virtues and the hadiths that imply their good qualities. And also a refutation of the Khawarij who say that Ali and those who followed him, and Mu'awiyah and those who sided with him, are disbelievers, because they committed the killing of a believer, which according to them is a major sin that expels from the boundary of faith.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'remaining on the truth' from the creed. It explains that the Companions remain on the truth and with the truth, without any change. This refutes the Rafidah who claim that the first three caliphs changed after the Prophet's death, despite the Quranic verses and hadith praising them. It also refutes the Khawarij who declare Ali and Muawiyah and their followers disbelievers for the killing of believers, because the Khawarij consider major sin as disbelief.

Key Terms
غابرين على الحق
الروافض
الخوارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'the three' refers to all Companions, but the context specifically addresses the first three caliphs.
Correction: The term 'الثلاثة' in the passage refers specifically to Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman, not all Companions.

Checkpoint

B) The Rafidah and the Khawarij

D) The Sufis and the philosophers

Key Takeaway
The Companions' constancy in truth is a Sunni doctrine that refutes both Rafidah and Khawarij.

On Speaking Well of the Companions
Defines the duty to mention the Companions only with good and provides proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the obligation to speak only good of the Companions and the theological reasoning behind it, including the principle of ijtihad and the authority of hadith and scholarly consensus.

Core Idea
The Companions must be mentioned only with good; any apparent fault is excused by their ijtihad and virtue.

This lesson addresses the proper attitude toward the Companions of the Prophet (ﷺ). It establishes the rule that we must only speak well of them, explains the rationale for excusing any apparent shortcomings, and provides proof from hadith and the statements of major imams. Understanding this helps safeguard one’s faith from sectarian bias and disrespect to those whom Allah has praised.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the opening rule and how it is reinforced with multiple proofs.
2
Understand the rationale for ijtihad
The author explains that even if some Companions did something that in form is evil, it was based on ijtihad and not persistence in sin. This is key to the Sunni position.
3
Examine the three hadith proofs
Identify each hadith: 'The best of generations is my generation', 'When my Companions are mentioned, refrain', and 'My Companions are like stars'. Reflect on how each supports the rule.
These hadiths establish the virtue and authority of the Companions.
4
Review the scholars' statements
Read the quotes from Ibn Daqiq al-Eid, al-Shafi'i, Ahmad, and Abu Hanifa. Notice how each reinforces the duty to speak well and refrain from criticism.
Ibn Daqiq's principle about doubtful reports is especially important.
5
Synthesize the lesson's message
Consider the overall consensus of Ahl al-Sunnah: the Companions are all upright, and we must only mention them with good. Reflect on how this applies to discussions today.
This lesson guards against disrespecting the Companions, a dangerous innovation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا نذكر الصحابة إلا بخير. ولا نذكر (الصحابة أي مجتمعين ومنفردين، وفي نسخة ولا نذكر أحداً من أصحاب رسول الله صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم إلا بخير) يعني وإن صدر من بعضهم بعضُ ما هو في الصورة شر، فإنه إما كان عن اجتهاد ولم يكن على وجه فساد من إصرار وعناد، بل كان رجوعهم عنه إلى خير معاد بناء على حسن الظن بهم، ولقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: ((خير القرون قرني)). ولقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: ((إذا ذكر أصحابي فأمسكوا)). ولذلك ذهب جمهور العلماء إلى أن الصحابة رضي الله عنهم كلهم عدول قبل فتنة عثمان وعلي وكذا بعدها، ولقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: ((أصحابي كالنجوم بأيهم اقتديتم اهتديتم)). وقال ابن دقيق العيد في عقيدته: وما نقل فيما شجر بينهم واختلفوا فيه فمنه ما هو باطل وكذب فلا يلتفت إليه، وما كان صحيحا أولناه تأويلا حسنا؛ لأن الثناء عليهم من الله سابق وما نقل من الكلام اللاحق محتمل للتأويل والمشكوك والموهوم لا يبطل المحقق والمعلوم. هذا، وقال الشافعي رحمه الله: «تلك دماء طهر الله أيدينا منها فلم نلوث ألسنتنا». وسئل أحمد عن أمر علي وعائشة رضي الله عنهما، فقال: ﴿ أَمَّةٌ قَدْ خَلَتْ لَهَا مَا كَسَبَتْ وَلَكُمْ مَّا كَسَبْتُمْ وَلَا تُسْلُونَ عَمَّا كَانُوا يَعْمَلُونَ ﴾ [البقرة: ١٣٤]، وقال أبو حنيفة رضي الله عنه: «لو لا علي لم نعرف السيرة في الخوارج».
And we do not mention the Companions except with good. And we do not mention (the Companions, i.e. collectively and individually, and in a copy: and we do not mention anyone of the Companions of the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, except with good) meaning, even if some of them did something that in form is evil, it was either based on ijtihad and not out of corruption from persistence and obstinacy, rather their return from it was to a good outcome based on thinking well of them, and because of his saying, peace and blessings be upon him: 'The best of generations is my generation.' And because of his saying, peace and blessings be upon him: 'When my Companions are mentioned, refrain.' Therefore, the majority of scholars held that the Companions, may God be pleased with them, are all trustworthy before the fitnah of Uthman and Ali and likewise after it, and because of his saying, peace and blessings be upon him: 'My Companions are like stars; whichever of them you follow, you will be guided.' And Ibn Daqiq al-Eid said in his creed: What has been narrated concerning what occurred among them and they differed about – some of it is false and lies, so no attention is paid to it, and what was authentic we interpret in a good way; because the praise of them from God is prior, and what has been narrated of later speech is subject to interpretation, and the doubtful and imaginary does not invalidate the certain and known. This, and al-Shafi'i, may God have mercy on him, said: 'Those are bloods from which God purified our hands, so we do not defile our tongues.' And Ahmad was asked about the matter of Ali and Aisha, may God be pleased with them both, and he said: 'That is a nation that has passed away; for them is what they earned, and for you is what you earned, and you will not be asked about what they used to do' [Al-Baqara 2:134]. And Abu Hanifa, may God be pleased with him, said: 'If not for Ali, we would not know the way of dealing with the Khawarij.'
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement and defense of the obligation to speak only good of the Companions. It begins by asserting the rule: we do not mention the Companions except with good. The author then explains that even if some actions of the Companions appear evil in form, they were based on ijtihad and not on persistence in sin; their subsequent return to good shows their righteousness. The author supports this with three hadiths: the best generation is his; when Companions are mentioned, refrain; and Companions are like stars. He then cites Ibn Daqiq al-Eid's principle that doubtful reports about disputes among them do not invalidate the established certainty of their virtue. Imam al-Shafi'i famously said he will not speak about those events. Imam Ahmad cited the Quranic verse leaving judgment to God. Finally, Abu Hanifa praised Ali for showing how to deal with the Khawarij. The overall argument is that the Companions are all upright (udul) and that the Ahl al-Sunnah refrains from criticizing them.

Key Terms
صحابة
عدول
اجتهاد
فتنة
ابن دقيق العيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the passage attributes infallibility to all Companions, meaning they could never make a mistake.
Correction: The author does not claim infallibility; rather, he argues that any apparent fault was due to ijtihad and not persistence in sin, and that their overall virtue is established by divine praise and hadith.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on mentioning disputes among the Companions?
A) They must be mentioned to learn from mistakes.
B) Only the agreed-upon good should be mentioned.
C) All reports are to be accepted as true.
D) The Companions are to be criticized for their errors.

Key Takeaway
The Ahl al-Sunnah's position is to speak only good of the Companions, regarding any apparent fault as either ijtihad or misinterpreted, and to refrain from discussing internal disputes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a criticism of a Companion, what is your immediate reaction? Do you defend them or stay silent? The hadith says 'when my Companions are mentioned, refrain.' How will you train yourself to hold your tongue?
?
Imam al-Shafi'i said: 'Those are bloods from which God purified our hands, so we do not defile our tongues.' Are you keeping your hands pure, or do you allow yourself to dwell on historical disputes? What specific step will you take today to avoid defiling your tongue?
?
Think of a time you spoke ill of a Companion, perhaps without realizing. How can you make amends and commit to only speaking good from now on? Write down one practical change in how you will discuss the Sahaba going forward.

The Ruling on Cursing Mu'awiya and Yazid
Sharh al-'Aqa'id quotation and commentary on cursing Mu'awiya and Yazid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced positions of Sunni scholars regarding cursing Companions involved in early Islamic conflicts, particularly Mu'awiya and Yazid, and the importance of evidence-based judgment.

Core Idea
Not all conflict among Companions justifies cursing; the Salaf demanded clear proof.

In this lesson, we examine a passage from Sharh al-'Aqa'id that distinguishes between different levels of reviling Companions and specifically addresses the cases of Mu'awiya and Yazid. The discussion highlights the methodological principle that cursing Muslims is prohibited unless there is definitive evidence of apostasy, which the Salaf did not find in these cases.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general ruling on slandering Companions
Read the quote from Sharh al-'Aqa'id which divides slandering into two categories: if it contradicts definitive evidence it is kufr, otherwise it is bid'a and fisq.
2
Note the implication for the two shaykhs
The commentator explicitly states that reviling Abu Bakr and Umar is not considered kufr according to the general body of scholars.
3
Examine the position on cursing Mu'awiya
The Salaf did not permit cursing Mu'awiya because his rebellion (bugha) does not necessitate cursing. Understand why rebellion alone is not grounds for curse.
4
Explore the difference of opinion on Yazid
Some scholars prohibited cursing Yazid based on the Prophet's prohibition of cursing those who pray. Others permitted it based on the claim that he ordered the killing of al-Husayn, which they considered kufr.
5
Reflect on the commentator's critique
The commentator notes that the argument for cursing Yazid is weak because the transmitter is ambiguous and the claim that Yazid ordered the killing lacks proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ففي "شرح العقائد : سب الصحابة والطعن فيهم إن كان مما يخالف الأدلة القطعية فكفر كقذف عائشة رضي الله عنها وإلا فبدعة وفسق، وهذا تصريح من العلامة أن سب الشيخين ليس بكفر عند العامة، ثم قال وبالجملة لم ينقل عن السلف المجتهدين والعلماء الصالحين جواز لعن معاوية وأحزابه؛ لأن غاية أمرهم البغي والخروج على الإمام الحق وهو لا يوجب اللعن. وإنما اختلفوا في يزيد بن معاوية حتى ذكر في "الخلاصة" وغيره أنه لا ينبغي اللعن عليه، أي ولا على الحجاج؛ لأن النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم نهى عن لعن المصلين ومن أهل القبلة، وما نقل من لعنه صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم لبعض أهل القبلة، فلما أنه يعلم من أحوال الناس ما لا يعلم غيره يعني فلعله كان منافقا أو علم أنه يموت كافراً. قال: وبعضهم أطلق اللعن عليه، أي على يزيد لما أنه كفر حين أمر بقتل الحسين رضي الله عنه، انتهى. ولا يخفى ما في نقله حيث أبهم في قائله، ثم تعليله يحتاج إلى إثبات أمره بقتل الحسين.
For in Sharḥ al-'Aqā'id: 'Reviling the Companions and attacking them: if it contradicts definitive evidence, it is disbelief, like slandering 'Ā'isha, may Allāh be pleased with her; otherwise it is innovation and transgression.' And this is an explicit statement from the scholar that reviling the two shaykhs is not disbelief according to the general public. Then he said: 'In summary, it has not been transmitted from the striving predecessors and righteous scholars the permissibility of cursing Mu'āwiya and his parties, because the utmost of their case is rebellion and revolt against the rightful imam, and that does not necessitate cursing. They only differed concerning Yazīd ibn Mu'āwiya, until it was mentioned in al-Khulāṣa and elsewhere that one should not curse him, nor al-Ḥajjāj, because the Prophet, may Allāh bless him and grant him peace, forbade cursing those who pray and those of the qibla. And what is transmitted of his cursing some of the people of the qibla is because he knew from the conditions of people what others do not know, meaning perhaps he was a hypocrite or he knew that he would die a disbeliever.' He said: 'And some of them declared it permissible to curse him, i.e., Yazīd, because he disbelieved when he ordered the killing of al-Ḥusayn, may Allāh be pleased with him.' End. And it is not hidden what is in his transmission, where he left the speaker ambiguous, then his reasoning requires proof that he ordered the killing of al-Ḥusayn.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the rulings of cursing certain historical figures. The commentator cites Sharḥ al-'Aqā'id, which distinguishes between slandering Companions: if it contradicts definitive evidence (like slandering 'Ā'isha), it is kufr; otherwise it is bid'a and fisq. This explicitly states that reviling the two shaykhs is not kufr according to the general body of scholars. Then the passage addresses cursing Mu'āwiya: the Salaf did not permit it because his rebellion does not necessitate cursing. Regarding Yazīd, there is a difference: some prohibit cursing based on the Prophet's prohibition of cursing those who pray, and any reported cursing by the Prophet is specific to his knowledge of hypocrisy or future disbelief. Others permitted cursing Yazīd based on the claim that he ordered the killing of al-Ḥusayn, but the commentator notes that this claim is unproven and the source is ambiguous. The passage thus guards against hasty takfīr and cursing without clear evidence.

Key Terms
سب الصحابة
لعن
معاوية
يزيد
الحسين
بغي
خروج على الإمام الحق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is permissible to curse any Companion who fought against 'Ali or was involved in the killing of al-Husayn.
Correction: The Salaf did not permit cursing Mu'awiya, and regarding Yazid, the evidence is contested; the commentator stresses the need for clear proof.

Checkpoint
According to the citation from Sharḥ al-'Aqā'id, what is the ruling on slandering a Companion in a way that contradicts definitive evidence?
A) It is merely innovation and transgression.
B) It is disbelief (kufr).
C) It is permissible if done for a valid reason.
D) It is only a minor sin.

Key Takeaway
The Salaf were cautious in cursing historical figures, requiring clear proof of apostasy; without such proof, cursing is not permitted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel anger towards a Muslim leader or historical figure, do you ever find yourself making judgments about their faith or cursing them in your heart? What does this lesson teach you about the danger of that, and how will you guard your tongue starting today?
?
The Salaf withheld curses even from those who rebelled against the rightful imam. Is there anyone in your life—a scholar, a leader, or a family member—whom you have mentally condemned without full evidence? How will you seek repentance and change your attitude this week?
?
This passage shows that even the claim that Yazid ordered the killing of al-Husayn is unproven. How often do you accept claims about others without verifying, especially when it confirms your biases? What specific step will you take this week to verify a rumor before believing or repeating it?

Cursing Companions: When is it Kufr?
Classification of cursing Companions based on contradiction to definitive proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic ruling on cursing the Companions and how it is classified based on whether it contradicts definitive proofs.

Core Idea
Cursing a Companion is not always kufr; it depends on what is said.

This lesson examines a passage from Sharh al-'Aqa'id that provides a nuanced ruling on cursing the Companions. It distinguishes between actions that amount to disbelief and those that are merely sinful innovations. The passage uses the example of accusing 'A'ishah to illustrate the boundary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the key points.
Notice the conditional 'if... then' structure.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage presents two rulings: kufr (if contradicts definitive proofs) and bid'ah/fisq (otherwise). Pinpoint the example given for kufr.
Accusing 'A'ishah is the paradigmatic case of kufr.
3
Note the ruling on the two shaykhs
Observe the implication that cursing Abu Bakr and 'Umar is not kufr according to mainstream, though still a grave sin.
This clarifies a common misconception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ففي "شرح العقائد : سب الصحابة والطعن فيهم إن كان مما يخالف الأدلة القطعية فكفر كقذف عائشة رضي الله عنها وإلا فبدعة وفسق، وهذا تصريح من العلامة أن سب الشيخين ليس بكفر عند العامة، ثم قال وبالجملة لم ينقل عن السلف المجتهدين والعلماء الصالحين جواز لعن معاوية وأحزابه؛ لأن غاية أمرهم البغي والخروج على الإمام الحق وهو لا يوجب اللعن. وإنما اختلفوا في يزيد بن معاوية حتى ذكر في "الخلاصة" وغيره أنه لا ينبغي اللعن عليه، أي ولا على الحجاج؛ لأن النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم نهى عن لعن المصلين ومن أهل القبلة، وما نقل من لعنه صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم لبعض أهل القبلة، فلما أنه يعلم من أحوال الناس ما لا يعلم غيره يعني فلعله كان منافقا أو علم أنه يموت كافراً. قال: وبعضهم أطلق اللعن عليه، أي على يزيد لما أنه كفر حين أمر بقتل الحسين رضي الله عنه، انتهى.
In Sharh al-'Aqa'id: Cursing the Companions and attacking them — if it contradicts definitive proofs, it is kufr, like accusing 'A'ishah (may Allah be pleased with her); otherwise it is bid'ah and fisq. This is a clear statement from the scholar that cursing the two shaykhs [Abu Bakr and 'Umar] is not kufr according to the general public. Then he said: In summary, it has not been transmitted from the early mujtahid scholars and righteous scholars that it is permissible to curse Mu'awiyah and his party, because the utmost of their matter is transgression and rebellion against the rightful imam, and that does not necessitate cursing. They only differed regarding Yazid ibn Mu'awiyah, until it was mentioned in al-Khulasa and other works that one should not curse him, nor al-Hajjaj, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade cursing those who pray and those who face the qibla. And what is narrated of his cursing some people of the qibla is because he knows of people's states what others do not know, meaning that perhaps he was a hypocrite or knew that he would die a disbeliever. He said: Some have permitted cursing him, i.e., Yazid, because he disbelieved when he ordered the killing of al-Husayn (may Allah be pleased with him). End quote.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a quotation from Sharh al-'Aqa'id that classifies the act of cursing the Companions. It distinguishes between actions that are kufr (like falsely accusing 'Aishah) and those that are merely bid'ah or fisq. It then states that cursing Abu Bakr and 'Umar is not kufr according to the mainstream. The discussion moves to Mu'awiyah and his party, noting that the Salaf did not permit cursing them because their offense was transgression, not kufr. The text then focuses on Yazid, noting a difference of opinion: some say not to curse him based on the Prophet's prohibition, while others permit it because he ordered the killing of al-Husayn, which they consider kufr. The passage ends with the phrase 'End quote', indicating the end of the excerpt from Sharh al-'Aqa'id.

Key Terms
سب الصحابة
طعن
الأدلة القطعية
بدعة
فسق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Cursing any Companion is automatically kufr.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only when the cursing contradicts definitive proofs does it become kufr; otherwise it is bid'ah and fisq.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does cursing a Companion become kufr?
A) When it is done publicly
B) When it contradicts definitive proofs
C) When it is directed at a prominent Companion
D) When it is repeated multiple times

Key Takeaway
Cursing a Companion is only kufr if it involves a statement that contradicts definitive Islamic proofs; otherwise it is a grave sin but not disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever spoken ill of a Companion in a way that you knew was based on false information from unreliable sources? How will you ensure your tongue is guarded from even minor disrespect of the Companions from now on?
?
This lesson teaches that cursing the two shaykhs is not kufr but still a grave sin. Does knowing that the ruling is less than kufr make you feel less concerned about avoiding such speech, or does it increase your awareness that every sin against the Companions is a serious matter in the sight of Allah?
?
If you were in a gathering where a person cursed a Companion, what would you do? Would you silently condemn it, speak up, or distance yourself? What practical step will you take this week to defend the honor of the Companions?

The Debate on Cursing Mu'awiyah and Yazid
Discussion of cursing Mu'awiyah and differing views on Yazid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference of opinion among scholars regarding cursing Mu'awiyah and Yazid, and the principles behind those rulings.

Core Idea
The Salaf did not permit cursing Mu'awiyah; scholars differed on Yazid due to the killing of al-Husayn.

This lesson continues the excerpt from Sharh al-'Aqa'id, which addresses the specific cases of Mu'awiyah and Yazid. The passage notes that the early scholars did not permit cursing Mu'awiyah, as his transgression did not warrant cursing. Regarding Yazid, there is a difference: some prohibit cursing based on a general prohibition, while others permit it due to his supposed disbelief in ordering the killing of al-Husayn.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully to understand the arguments for each position.
Note the phrase 'وبالجملة' which signals the summary.
2
Understand the ruling on Mu'awiyah
Why did the Salaf not permit cursing Mu'awiyah? Identify the reason given: his offense is transgression, not kufr.
The passage says 'لا يوجب اللعن' — transgression does not necessitate cursing.
3
Analyze the two views on Yazid
Distinguish the opinion that prohibits cursing Yazid (based on the Prophet's prohibition) from the opinion that permits it (due to his order killing al-Husayn considered kufr).
The first opinion also extends to al-Hajjaj.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This is a clear statement from the scholar that cursing the two shaykhs [Abu Bakr and 'Umar] is not kufr according to the general public. He said: Some have permitted cursing him, i.e., Yazid, because he disbelieved when he ordered the killing of al-Husayn (may Allah be pleased with him). End quote.

This passage is a quotation from Sharh al-'Aqa'id that classifies the act of cursing the Companions. It distinguishes between actions that are kufr (like falsely accusing 'Aishah) and those that are merely bid'ah or fisq. It then states that cursing Abu Bakr and 'Umar is not kufr according to the mainstream. يزيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All scholars agree that Yazid should be cursed.
Correction: The passage shows that there is a difference of opinion; some scholars prohibit cursing Yazid based on the Prophet's prohibition.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did the Salaf not permit cursing Mu'awiyah?
A) Because he was a Companion
B) Because his offense was only transgression, not kufr
C) Because he repented
D) Because the Prophet forbade cursing him

Key Takeaway
Scholars differed on cursing Yazid: some prohibited it due to a general prohibition on cursing Muslims, while others permitted it due to his order to kill al-Husayn, which they considered disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about historical figures like Yazid, what is your immediate reaction? Do you find yourself quick to curse or condemn based on secondhand reports, or do you restrain your tongue as the Salaf did? This lesson teaches that even the scholars differed. Before you speak, have you taken the time to understand the full picture? Commit to not uttering a curse against any Muslim without knowledge.
?
The Prophet forbade cursing those who pray and face the qibla. But do you find yourself cursing people in your life—family, leaders, or people of a different sect? The passage reminds that our knowledge is limited; only Allah knows what is in the heart. Can you truly justify cursing someone when you do not know their final state? Make a decision today to stop cursing anyone, and instead pray for guidance.
?
The scholars who permitted cursing Yazid did so because they saw his action as kufr. But note the careful reasoning they employed: they did not take the matter lightly. Are you prone to hastily declaring others as disbelievers based on actions you dislike? How can you apply the same caution and reliance on evidence as the scholars in this passage? Resolve to refrain from takfir and to speak only with knowledge.

The Necessity of Proof Before Accusation
Author criticizes a report and cites Ghazali on verification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author critiques a previous transmission for lacking clear attribution and why Ghazali insists on definitive proof before accusing a Muslim of a major sin.

Core Idea
Accusing a Muslim of a major sin requires certain knowledge, not speculation.

The author begins by pointing out the weakness of a previous report—its source is vague and its reasoning rests on unproven premises. To reinforce this, he quotes Ghazali's 'Ihya' which lays down a foundational principle: one cannot attribute a heinous act like killing al-Husayn to Yazid without clear evidence. Ghazali distinguishes between established historical facts and unverified claims, cautioning against reckless accusations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's critique
Notice that the author first criticizes the earlier transmission for being vague about its speaker and for requiring unproven premises. This sets up the need for verification.
2
Examine Ghazali's ruling
Read the quote from Ghazali. He says that since it is not established that Yazid killed or ordered the killing of al-Husayn, one cannot even say he did, let alone curse him. He contrasts this with verified cases like the killers of 'Ali and 'Umar.
Ghazali uses the term 'tawatur' (widespread transmission) to describe what constitutes proof.
3
Understand the principle
Ghazali concludes that it is forbidden to accuse any Muslim of fisq (wickedness) or kufr without verification. This is the core lesson: accusations require certainty.
4
Reflect on the danger
The author then notes Ghazali's summary: cursing specific individuals is dangerous and should be avoided; even refraining from cursing Iblis is safer.
This highlights that caution is preferable even in cases of known evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يخفى ما في نقله حيث أبهم في قائله، ثم تعليله يحتاج إلى إثبات أمره بقتل الحسين رضي الله عنه أولاً ثم ترتب كفره عليه ثانياً، وكلاهما ممنوع. فقد قال حجة الإسلام في "الإحياء": «فإن قيل: هل يجوز لعن يزيد؛ لأنه قاتل الحسين أو آمر به؟ قلنا هذا لم يثبت أصلا، فلا يجوز أن يقال: إنه قتله أو أمر به فضلا عن اللعنة، لأنه لا تجوز نسبة مسلم إلى كبيرة من غير تحقيق. نعم يجوز أن يقال : قتل ابن ملجم عليا رضي الله عنه وقتل أبو لؤلؤة عمر رضي الله عنه، فإن ذلك ثبت متواترا... فلا يرمى مسلم بفسق أو كفر من غير تحقيق». وعلى الجملة ففي لعن الأشخاص خطر فليجتنب، ولا خطر في السكوت عن لعن إبليس فضلا عن غيره، انتهى. ولأن الأمر بقتل الحسين رضي الله عنه لا يوجب الكفر، فإن قتل غير الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام كبيرة عند أهل السنة والجماعة إلا أن يكون مستحلا، وهو غير مختص بالحسين ونحوه مع أن الاستحلال أمر لا يطلع عليه إلا ذو الجلال، وإنما كان قتله نظير قتل عمار بن ياسر. وأما ما تفوه به بعض الجهلة من أن الحسين كان باغيا فباطل عند أهل السنة والجماعة، ولعل هذا من هذيانات الخوارج عن الجادة. ثم قال: واتفقوا على جواز اللعن على من قتله أو أمر به أو أجازه أو رضي به، ففيه بحث، لأنه مع كونه بظاهره مناقضا لما قدمه من بيان الخلاف إن أراد جواز اللعن الإجمالي بأن يقال: لعنة الله على قاتل الحسين أو الراضي به، فلا كلام فيه لقوله تعالى : أَلَا لَعْنَةُ اللهِ عَلَى الظَّلِمِينَ
It is not hidden what is in his transmission where he is vague about its speaker, then his reasoning requires proving his order to kill al-Husayn (may Allah be pleased with him) first, and then deducing his disbelief from it second, and both are prevented. For Hujjat al-Islam said in al-Ihya': 'If it is said: Is it permissible to curse Yazid because he killed al-Husayn or ordered it? We say: This has not been established at all, so it is not permissible to say that he killed him or ordered it, let alone cursing him, because it is not permissible to attribute a major sin to a Muslim without verification. Yes, it is permissible to say that Ibn Muljam killed 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) and Abu Lu'lu' killed 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him), for that is established by tawatur... So a Muslim should not be accused of fisq or kufr without verification.' And in summary, in cursing individuals there is danger, so it should be avoided, and there is no danger in refraining from cursing Iblis, let alone others. End quote. And because the order to kill al-Husayn (may Allah be pleased with him) does not necessitate disbelief, for killing non-prophets is a major sin according to Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah unless one considers it lawful, and it is not specific to al-Husayn and the like, and considering it lawful is something only the Majestic One knows. Rather, his killing is analogous to the killing of 'Ammar ibn Yasir. As for what some ignorant people have uttered that al-Husayn was a rebel (baghi), it is false according to Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, and perhaps this is from the deliriums of the Khawarij straying from the path. Then he said: And they agreed on the permissibility of cursing the one who killed him or ordered it or permitted it or was pleased with it, but there is discussion: because despite it apparently contradicting what he previously presented of the statement of disagreement, if he intends the permissibility of general cursing by saying: 'The curse of Allah be upon the killer of al-Husayn or the one pleased with it,' then there is no argument, because of His saying: 'Surely the curse of Allah is upon the wrongdoers.'
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a critique and further analysis by the author. He first criticizes the previous report (from Sharh al-'Aqa'id) for being vague about its source and for requiring unproven premises. He then cites Ghazali's Ihya' to support the view that one cannot attribute killing or ordering the killing of al-Husayn to Yazid without proof, and thus cannot curse him specifically. Ghazali distinguishes between cases established by tawatur (like the killers of 'Ali and 'Umar) and unproven accusations. The author then gives his own reasoning: ordering the killing of al-Husayn does not necessitate kufr because killing a non-prophet is a major sin, not kufr, unless one considers it lawful. He also refutes the claim that al-Husayn was a rebel. Finally, he addresses the statement that 'they agreed on permissibility of cursing the killer...' and explains that if it means general cursing (like 'curse of Allah upon the killer of al-Husayn'), it is permissible based on the Quranic verse. Thus, the passage clarifies the nuanced ruling on cursing specific individuals versus general condemnation.

Key Terms
لعن
يزيد
الحسين
تواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a historical report exists, it can be used to curse a person without verifying its chain or content.
Correction: Ghazali and the author insist that only facts established by tawatur or rigorous evidence can be used for accusations; otherwise it is forbidden.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali consider it permissible to mention that Ibn Muljam killed 'Ali but not that Yazid killed al-Husayn?
A) Because Ibn Muljam confessed
B) Because the killing of 'Ali is established by tawatur, while Yazid's involvement is not
C) Because 'Ali was a caliph and al-Husayn was not
D) Because the Quran mentions the killing of 'Ali

Key Takeaway
Accusing a Muslim of a major sin or disbelief requires certain proof; speculation is forbidden.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a negative report about a fellow Muslim, do you immediately assume it's true? How does this passage's demand for certain proof challenge your natural inclination to believe such reports without verification? What one habit can you change to guard your tongue?
?
Have you ever felt comfortable cursing a historical figure because the narrative seemed consistent? After reading Ghazali's words, can you honestly say you have avoided suspicion and guarded your speech? What will you do differently the next time you hear such an accusation?
?
The passage says there is no danger in refraining from cursing even Iblis. How does that statement confront your attitude towards sinners—are you still quick to condemn those who sin publicly? What would it mean for you to prioritize silence over accusation in your daily interactions, starting today?

Cursing Yazid: General vs. Specific Condemnation
Author gives his own reasoning and ruling on cursing Yazid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that ordering the killing of al-Husayn is a major sin, not disbelief, and the ruling on cursing specific individuals versus general condemnation.

Core Idea
General curses against wrongdoers are allowed, but cursing specific individuals requires caution and does not entail disbelief.

The author continues his analysis by addressing a key question: does ordering the killing of al-Husayn constitute disbelief? He clarifies that killing a non-prophet is a major sin, not kufr, unless one considers it lawful (istihlal). He also refutes the false claim that al-Husayn was a rebel. Finally, he addresses a statement about consensus on cursing the killer, distinguishing between specific and general curses.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the distinction: major sin vs. disbelief
The author states that ordering the killing of al-Husayn does not necessitate kufr. Killing a non-prophet is a major sin, but it becomes kufr only if one believes it is lawful. This is a key theological nuance.
2
Refute the 'baghi' claim
The author dismisses the idea that al-Husayn was a rebel (baghi) as false and attributes it to the Khawarij. This defends the honor of the Prophet's grandson.
3
Understand the ruling on cursing
The author discusses the statement that 'they agreed on permissibility of cursing the killer...' He explains that if it means a general curse like 'curse of Allah upon the killer of al-Husayn', it is permissible based on Quran 7:44. But specific cursing of individuals remains problematic.
The author points out a contradiction if the statement meant specific cursing.

The Ruling on Cursing Yazeed Without Proof
Al-Ghazali's ruling on cursing Yazeed with conditions of proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ghazali prohibits cursing Yazeed based on lack of proof and the principle of not attributing sin without verification.

Core Idea
Cursing a Muslim without proof is a greater sin than the alleged sin itself.

This passage from Sharh al-Fiqh al-Akbar addresses a sensitive historical issue: whether it is permissible to curse Yazeed ibn Muawiya for his alleged role in the killing of al-Husayn. The author quotes Imam al-Ghazali's clear ruling that such an act cannot be proven, and therefore cursing is not allowed. Let us examine the text and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ghazali quote
Focus on the question and answer structure. Note that the claim about Yazeed is unproven.
Tawatur (mass transmission) is key to establishing certainty in historical reports.
2
Understand the prohibition
Al-Ghazali prohibits cursing because it involves accusing a Muslim of a major sin without certain knowledge.
3
Compare with proven cases
The cases of Ibn Muljam and Abu Lu'lu'a are proven by tawatur, so it is permissible to attribute the acts to them.
This shows the standard required.
4
Reflect on the danger of cursing
The author concludes that avoiding cursing individuals is safer, even for Iblis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد قال حجة الإسلام في "الإحياء": «فإن قيل: هل يجوز لعن يزيد؛ لأنه قاتل الحسين أو آمر به؟ قلنا هذا لم يثبت أصلا، فلا يجوز أن يقال: إنه قتله أو أمر به فضلا اللعنة، لأنه لا تجوز نسبة مسلم إلى كبيرة من غير تحقيق. نعم يجوز أن يقال : قتل ابن ملجم عليا رضي الله عنه وقتل أبو لؤلؤة عمر رضي الله عنه، فإن ذلك ثبت متواتر (١).. أن يرمى مسلم بفسق أو كفر من غير تحقيق». وعلى الجملة ففي لعن الأشخاص خطر فليجتنب، ولا خطر في السكوت عن لعن إبليس فضلا عن غيره، انتهى. ولأن الأمر بقتل الحسين رضي الله عنه لا يوجب الكفر، فإن قتل غير الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام كبيرة عند أهل السنة والجماعة إلا أن يكون مستحلا، وهو غير مختص بالحسين ونحوه مع أن الاستحلال أمر لا يطلع عليه إلا ذو الجلال، وإنما كان قتله نظير قتل عمار بن ياسر. وأما ما تفوه به بعض الجهلة من أن الحسين كان باغيا فباطل عند أهل السنة والجماعة، ولعل هذا من هذيانات الخوارج عن الجادة. (١) وقع هاهنا في نسخة شرح الفقه الأكبر الشائعة في بلادنا تحريف شديد فنقل فيها لفظ الإحياء هكذا: بل لا يجوز أن يقال إن ابن ملجم قتل عليا ولا أبو لؤلؤ قتل عمر فإن ذلك لم يثبت متواترا.هـ، وهو باطل كما لا يخفى، والصواب ما نقلتُ فليتنبه. (الزمزمة القمرية، ص: ٢٧)
The Proof of Islam said in al-Ihya: 'If it is said: Is it permissible to curse Yazeed because he killed al-Husayn or commanded it? We say: This has not been established at all, so it is not permissible to say that he killed him or commanded it, let alone curse him, because it is not permissible to attribute a major sin to a Muslim without verification. Yes, it is permissible to say: Ibn Muljam killed Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, and Abu Lu'lu'a killed Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, for that is established as mutawatir. ... that a Muslim be accused of wrongdoing or disbelief without verification.' In sum, in cursing individuals there is danger, so one should avoid it, and there is no danger in refraining from cursing Iblis, let alone others. End. And because commanding the killing of al-Husayn, may Allah be pleased with him, does not necessitate disbelief, for killing other than prophets, peace be upon them, is a major sin according to Ahl al-Sunna wal-Jama'a unless one considers it lawful, and that is not specific to al-Husayn and his likes, moreover considering it lawful is a matter that only the Majestic knows. Rather his killing was analogous to the killing of Ammar ibn Yasir. As for what some ignoramuses uttered that al-Husayn was a rebel, that is false according to Ahl al-Sunna wal-Jama'a, and perhaps this is from the delirious ramblings of the Khawarij who deviated from the path. (1) Here, in a copy of Sharh al-Fiqh al-Akbar widespread in our country, there is a severe corruption, in which the wording of al-Ihya was transmitted as: 'Rather, it is not permissible to say that Ibn Muljam killed Ali nor that Abu Lu'lu' killed Umar, for that is not established as mutawatir.' Etc. That is false, as is not hidden, and the correct is what I transmitted, so let one be attentive. (al-Zamzama al-Qamariyya, p. 27)
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents a theological argument regarding the ruling on cursing Yazeed ibn Muawiya. It begins by quoting al-Ghazali's Ihya, where he addresses the question of whether it is permissible to curse Yazeed for his alleged involvement in the killing of al-Husayn. Al-Ghazali states that the accusation has not been proven, so one cannot say that Yazeed killed or ordered the killing, let alone curse him. He analogizes that cursing a Muslim without verification is impermissible, but notes that it is permissible to say that Ibn Muljam killed Ali and Abu Lu'lu'a killed Umar because those are established by tawatur. The author then adds his own reasoning, explaining that even if the order to kill al-Husayn were proven, it would not constitute kufr, because killing a non-prophet is only a major sin (kabira) unless one considers it lawful (istihlal), which is an internal matter known only to Allah. The author then refutes the claim that al-Husayn was a baghi (rebel), stating it is false and attributes it to the Khawarij. Finally, the editor's note corrects a corrupted version of the Ihya quote that wrongly claimed the deaths of Ali and Umar were not mutawatir.

Key Terms
لعن
متواتر
كبيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is permissible to curse Yazeed because of his evil actions, even if not proven.
Correction: The passage teaches that cursing requires certain knowledge; without proof, it is prohibited to even attribute the act, let alone curse.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali, why is it permissible to say that Ibn Muljam killed Ali but not that Yazeed killed al-Husayn?
A) Because Ibn Muljam's act is proven by tawatur, while Yazeed's is not.
B) Because Ibn Muljam was a known enemy of Ali, while Yazeed was a caliph.
C) Because the killing of Ali was more historically significant.
D) Because Yazeed's involvement is well-known but not mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
One must not curse a Muslim without proof, as it involves accusing them of a major sin without certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you spoke negatively about someone—whether a historical figure or a living person—based on hearsay. How does al-Ghazali's insistence on 'certain knowledge' before cursing a Muslim expose the gravity of your words? Will you repent from that and commit to only speaking with certainty?
?
Is there a Muslim individual you have privately cursed in your heart, perhaps a public figure you dislike? According to this lesson, you are attributing a major sin to them without proof. What would it take for you to stop that habit right now?
?
You may not curse Yazeed aloud, but do you harbor contempt in your heart based on popular narratives? If the evidence is not mutawatir, your contempt is unjustified. Can you truly say you have no such hidden enmity? What will you do to cleanse your heart?
